THE BED PAGE OF AUSTRALASIAN NOTES AND QUERIES .
[lt ia desired to oolleot information from all manner of
readers and observers on all manner of curious and in-
teresting subjects. A not-exolusive preference will be
given to notes and queries especially Australasian, or kin
with Australasian, in their origin and reference —parti-
cularly to those dealing with aboriginal life and language,
natural history, white-Australasian language, customs,
exploits and typical developments, characteristic old-
time anecdotes, and so on. Brevity of statement is
essential. Writers must observe the'ordinary correspon-
dence conditions. The help of BullsriN readers every-
where is asked in recording some of many valuable facts,
impressions, or experiences which may otherwise be lost
to our country. Notes may be forwarded on any interest-
ing topio—not necessarily relatiug to something already
in print. Queries may be suggested; and any relevant
information will'be welcome in reply, even if not precisely
answering. Contributions must be endorsed ‘Notes and
Queries,' and if dealing with matters previously referred-
to the number of note or query and date of issue must be
added. All references to books should state title, author,
publisher, date of publication, place where published,
and pages quoted from ; and all records of personal
experience should give date and place of occurrence
(province, district, miles from nearest post-town), and
other helpful details.]
3. —Whistling Spears.
Spears whistle and spears don’t. It all depends
on the shape and make of the weapon, and the
manner in which it is thrown. A straight,
smooth, inflexible spear, thrown so that it has an
even course, and but one motion, makes no noise
—unless it passes very close to the ear, when the
swish of the wind may be heard ; but a weak,
pliable spear must certainly make a sound on
account of its quivering motion through the air.
Crooked and knotty spears will also whiz. In
like manner a short straight stick thrown end on
to the ground, so that it will revolve endwise in a
straight course, will cause a loud whirring sound.
A rough stone or pebble will make the same
sound; but a very round, Bmooth one is silent
until it has touched something.—E.S.S.
There are spears and spears; ears and ears!
‘Scotty the Wrinkler ’ gives the scientific reason
why spears make a noise, which is practically
(from my standpoint) that ‘Nature abhors a
vacuum’ and, consequently, Dame Nature,
being essentially feminine, emits a little shriek
or whistle when assaulted by the aforesaid
vaouum at the tail of the spear. It _is
» fact beyond controversy that all missiles
and projectiles are accompanied by a sound more
or less audible during their flight. A case in
point. I was in charge of an exploring expedition
in the days when Cairns and the Hodgkinson
were just in the egg. We had passed through a
part of the Trinity Bay coast country with-
out coming into close quarters with the
natives until we reached one of their winter
camps -on the high bank of a river, several
large grass-thatched huts being an interesting
and conspicuous feature. The tribe vacated the
camp on our approach, and we left the place,
making for the plains without disturbing their
goods and chattels. On the skirt of a broad plain
fwo blacks were seen making signs which we
interpreted as pacific responding in similar
terms; but immediately we with our horses
oarae near them, they scuttled away time
after time until our patience was exhausted.
The physical conditions of the country being
unfavorable to an ambush, I offered _ to
¥ake an individual attempt at an interview.
he rest of my party then walked slowly away,
and, as they retreated, the two blacks (abso-
lutely naked and waving their hands with ges-
tures of amity) approaohed the spot where I re-
mained mounted upon my horse. The grass was
short —a little above the knees of the two niggers.
Phey were apparently unarmed —no oover for
other members of the tribe being possible for at
least half a mile; so I allowed the two to get
within forty yards of me. My rifle rested in the
* bucket,’ and my revolver in its holster, as I cer-
tainly had no reason to suspect treachery of any
kind. Suddenly, as if by preconcerted signal,
each black made a quick, upward move-
ment of the right foot, lifting to the right
hand a spear (which had been trailed along
by the toes, concealed by the grass). The
two spears were thrown fair at me (unfair, I
■aid) at the same moment. I threw myself quickly
backwards on my horse’s back, and the spears
passed within a few inches of my body. If no
other spears in the world whistle, I solemnly
swear these two did —and so did I, with relief,
when I got away ! The rest of the story has no
ooncern here; but I brought those two spears
back with me—they were of beautiful workman-
ship, as is the case with all the coast weapons of
Central Q. ; and were each barbed with about 20
stings of the sting-ray or stingaree.-
SAQOHS.
L. FKRD.
This extraot applies
The ’ shrieking shrapnel,’ of whioh one reads in the
description of every battle, did not sound to me so much
like a shriek as it did like the jarring sound of telegraph
wires when someone strikes the pole from whieh they hang
(writes a correspondent of the ‘Times,’ who was at the
Velestine fight between the Greeks and the Turks). When
they passed very Close, the noise was like the rushing
that rises between two railroad-trains when they pass
each other in opposite directions and at great speed.
After a few hours, we learned by observation that when
we heard a shell sound overhead it had already struck
somewhere beyond us. which was comforting, and which
is explained, of course, by the fact that the speed of the
shell is so much greater than the rate at which sound
travels. We found the bullets much more disturbing ;
they seemed to be less open in their warfare, and to steal
up and sneak by, leaving no sign, and only whispering as
they passed. They moved under a cloak of invisibility,
and made one feel as though he were a blind man in a
game of blind-man’s-buff, where everyone tapped him m
passing, leaving him puzzled and ignorant as to whither
they had gone and from what point they would come
next. The bullets sounded like rustling silk, or like
humming-birds on a warm summer’s day, or like the wind
as it is imitated on the stage of a theatre. Anyone who
has stood behind the scenes when a storm is progressing
on the stage knows the little wheel wound with silk that
brushes against another piece of silk, and which produces
the whistling effect of the wind. At Velestino, when the
firing was very heavy, it was exactly as though someone
were turning one of these silk-wheels, and so rapidly as
to make the whistling continuous. —X. A.M.
This disoussion closes with the affirmation that in MI
probability any object moving through any medium
causes a sound-vibration or a vibration akin to sound-
vibration. A paper-boat floated by a zephyr is as noisy
in proportion to its size and speed as a line-of-battle ship
propelled by 15,000 h.p. And it is likely that Lorenzo
was right—
• There’s not the smallest orb which thou behold’st
But in his motion like an angel sings,
Still quiring to the young-eyed cherubim }
Such harmony is in immortal souls;
But whilst this muddy vesture of decay
Both grossly close it in, we cannot hear it.’
Even in vacuo, despite ‘Scotty,’ the ether-disturbance
set up by a moving object probably makes a noise audible
by ears like those of Balder, who, as Norse legends
declare, could hear the grass grow.
Still, in any fair connotation, a * sound ’ is limited
by the range of vibrations which the best human ears
oan distinguish—and at the best times ; for the power of
hearing not only varies in different persons, but varies
from time to time in the same person according to phy-
sical condition. It is quite evident that the passage
through the atmosphere of a spear must cause sound-
vibrations, which will be audible or inaudible according
to the strength of the vibrations and the keenness of the
observer’s hearing. And obviously a thin, rigid spear,
cleaving the air with the least possible shock, is lees
likely to cause audible sound-vibrations than a thick, or
flexible, or barbed spear—just as a narrow-stemmed
torpedo - boat will out through water with hardly
a ripple as compared with the waves sent wallow-
ing from a bluff-bowed collier. Obviously, again,
the question whether any particular spear whistles is
settled by one person with ears keen enough to hear, and
not by fifty people whose ears are so dull that they do not
hear it. But this much is certain : the whistle (i.e., the
sound-vibration) is there; and, if we do not hear it, the
fault is in ‘ this muddy vesture of decay.’
39. —The Australian Flag.
The Australian flag was the emblem of the
‘Australian League,’ whioh sprang from ‘the
most important- colonial agitation of modern
times,’ the agitation to effect the abolition of the
transportation of conviots to Australia. Harsh
and unnatural indeed, at this time, was the voioe
of the British authorities. Tasmania was told
that millions had been spent in preparing her for
convicts : did her free inhabitants expect that at
their asking transportation should cease? Colo-
nies established as free might fairly object to the
influx of criminals ; Tasmania could not—convict-
ism marked her birth, and a foul oversea prison she
must remain. In her weakness she stretched forth
her hands to her sister provinces, asking for sym-
pathy and help. Nor was the appeal made in vain.
Her delegates came, and found Australia united
to face a common danger. As ‘ the beginning of
the end,’ the * Australasian League ’ was founded
at Melbourne, February 1, 1851. ‘lmmediately
on signing the league, a spleudid silken banner
was unfurled. It displayed the Union Jack on a
rich mazarine ground, with golden stars repre-
senting the Southern Cross, with the words,
“Australasian League, Victoria. Established
1851.” ’ Let one of the delegates, John West, the
historian of Tasmania, tell what followed The
mayor and corporation of Melbourne . . . pre-
sented the Tasmanian delegates with an address
... A banner, bought by general subscription,
was committed to their charge as a present to the
colonists of Tasmania. The ladies of Victoria
graced the ceremony of presentation. In giving
this beautiful emblem of Australian reunion,
“Gentlemen,” said the mayor, “I pray you to
receive it in the name of the people of Port Phil-
lip, and may it remain nailed to the mast until
the oolonies are emancipated from convictism.”
“We accept it with gratitude,” they replied,
“ may the flag which adorns it ever float above us
in mild sovereignty.” ’ The Tasmanian delegates
returning home, ‘ the banner entrusted to their
care was publicly delivered at a meeting, of which
Mr. Dry was chairman;’ and, says West in a
footnote, ‘the Raven, Captain Bell, was the first
vessel that carried the League flag, now floating
over every sea.’ (West’s History of Tasmania,
vui. i., p. 309.)-
VILLA-MA-NATA.
40. — Geographers’ Eccentricities.
The mistakes made in current geographies of
Australia, even of the latest date, are somewhat
amusing. For example, the description of N.S.W.
in a ‘ General Geography ’ by Hugh Robert Mill,
published by Macmillan and Co., commences :
“The parent colony of New South Wales has a
name disliked by the inhabitants, who wish to
change it for Australia." Certainly one distin-
guished citizen, no longer with us, wished to call
it Australia, but, so far as is known, nobody else
From a * Manual of Australasian Geography,’
published by William Collins and Co. : ‘ The
Lachlan, which rises in Queensland, and enters
N.S. Wales under the name of the Barwan.’ This
mistake would be pardonable in the case of a small
river, but not with regard to the Lachlan. This
book has a number of other minor errors, nob im-
portant enough to cull.
From ‘ A New Geography,’ by A. Sutherland,
M.A., published in Melbourne: ‘Every river
brings down a very small quantity of salt with it,
and the salt which Cooper’s Creek brings down to
Lake Eyre has been gathering for many centuries,
so that the lake is now intensely salt.’ This is
probably the most astounding and romantio theory
concerning salt lakes ever printed, and may rank
with Hans Andersen’s fairy tale, ‘ Why the sea is
salt.’ Sutherland, by the way, is very shaky all
through. ‘ The Victoria River in Northern Aus-
tralia flows into Cambridge Gulf.’. (?) The Great
Dividing Range ‘ runs from Princess Charlotte
Bay close to the eastern coast, through Queens-
land,’&c. The Great Dividing Range, as most
people who are interested in the matter know,
turns abruptly south of west, skirting the heads
of Carpentarian waters. The nearest point of its
approach to Princess Charlotte’s Bay is 300 miles
as the crow flies. Sutherland also states that the
G.D.R. is 2000 miles long. Considering that it
girdles three-fourths of the continent, GOOO would
be nearer the mark.-
-Wombat.
41. — Gibbets.
In a recent note on Villon it was queried whether
the accompanying illustration of an old French
gibbet was more or less imaginary. Some of
these structures used to be of quite important
architecture. In Violet-le- Due’s ‘ Dictionaire
Raisonnde de l’Architeoture Fran§aise ’ there
are several illustrations of gibbets constructed
of substantial masonry columns elevated on
a similar foundation. A double row of strong
timbers was placed across the top, from which
the victims were suspended by chains. They
remained there till they fell to bits, or till
their places were required for fresh candidates,
when they were cast into the common receptacle
for such rubbish in the centre of the monument.
The centre of this was a sort of charnel-house,
reached by a door in the foundation. In Hugo’s
‘Notre-Dame,’ speaking of the fate of Quasimodo,
it is said that long years after a skeleton of a
deformed nature was found clasping another,
which seemed that of a young woman —a curious
point being that the neck of the deformed one did
not seem to have been broken. They were found
in the pit, where La Esmeralda had been oast,
and where the faithful dwarf had found her and
died in her arms.-
WESTRALIAN.
42. —The Ball before Waterloo.
‘ The historical ball on the eve of the battle of
Waterloo.’ This erroneous phrase occurs con-
stantly in newspapers and magazines. The pro-
gramme of the Waterloo week was as follows :
On Thursday, June 15, 1815, the Duchess of
Richmond gave a ball at Brussels, to which many
English offioers wer<j invited, and from which they
went away in the early dawn (the sun must have
risen about 4 a.m.) to join the army. This is ‘ the
historical ball ’ so often mentioned and immor-
talised by Byron in lines which innumerable
schoolboys have learned to recite. On Friday,
June 16, there were two battles —one, English v.
French, at Quatre Bras ; the other, Prussians v.
French, at Ligny. On Saturday, June 17, the
English and Prussian armies were both retreating
northward — i.e. , nearer to Brussels. 1 inally on
Sunday, June 18, came Waterloo. Thus the
memorable ball took place on the third night before
Waterloo. That this error should be so deep-
seated in the popular mind is remarkable, because
Thackeray in ‘Vanity Fair’ (and surely Thackeray
oannot be already forgotten) gives a vivid des-
cription of the ball ; of the excitement and terror
at Brussels on Friday when the noise of the guns
at Quatre Bras was first heard ; of the compara-
tive lull on Saturday ; and then of the intense ex-
citement when the louder, because, less distant,
thunder of the cannonade at Waterloo resounded
through the city.-
SCRUTATOR.
43. —Mirages.
The following notice of a mirage at Blaokheath,
73 miles w. of Sydney, and 3494 feet above Sydney
level, appears in Sydney papers of 3/8/97 —
Blackhbath. August 2.
A remarkable mirage has been visible from the highest
elevations at Blaokheath, affording by the aid of a field'
glass an interesting glimpse of Sydney by night and
bringing into view apparently some miles of the city’s
gaslights. The phenomenon has occurred thrice in the
past seven nights. Monday, the 26th ultimo, was the
first occasion when it was observed, the phenomenon
being most complete in detail and also in actual beauty.
The repetitions occurred on Saturday and Sunday nights.
They were both tolerably distinct.
The weather at the time was fine; and the wind is
set down on one occasion as ‘somewhat Westerly.
OPTIC.
8. —She Oak.
The terra she oak as applied in Australia to
* colonial beer * dates back to 1820. About that
time a brewery was established on what was then
known as She Oak Hill, opposite old Government
House, Hobart. The owner, when asked how he
made such good beer, replied, ‘I put she oak in
it.’ At that''time the interprovincial trade of
Australia was confined to ‘Port Jackson (Syd-
ney) ‘Port Dalrymple ’ (Launceston) and ‘lhe
Derwent ’ (Hobart)—so the term became general.
M.D.
83.— Effect of Moonrays.
I was quartered at Etshowe, Zululand, in 88.
At the same place was stationed a detachment of
Mansel’s police. One of their subalterns (by name
H.) went to sleep, leaving the fly of his tent open,
and allowing the rays of the moon (at that time
full) to fall on his face. The effect was extra?
ordinary. On the following morning the whole
of the left side of his face looked as if it had been
melted down and then violently twitched in a
lateral and upward direction. His left eye was
completely closed ; nose all askew ; and mouth
twisted like an inverted comma. Nothing visible
of his right eye except the white. He was hardly
able to articulate, but made us understand that
he was in no pain, but felt queer and stiff. H'is
complexion, previously a bronze-red, was now of
a ghastly livid pallor in the affected parts. Al-
though a bit ‘dotty ’ at first, the effect gradually
wore off, and in about five weeks’ time his features
quite recovered their normal aspect.-
ZINGARO.
17. —Habits of Persons Lost in Bush.
Re the contention that men lost in the bush
travel in a circle to the left because the right leg
is the longest. A few years ago, whilst living on
a selection in Riverina, I had occasion to visit a
neighbor’s some six miles distant; and grass being
plentiful—consequently horses ‘ demons ’ to catch
—I resolved to walk. Intervening was a belt of
belar and pine-forest, rather more than two miles
wide, and locally termed the ‘ barber’s shop ’ —
presumably because it was so thick that anyone
riding through it came out with very little hair
left. Although the sky was cloudy and the sun
completely obscured, I didn’t for a moment doubt
my ability to make a short cut through. After
travelling briskly for threequarters of an hour or
so, I came to the opening at the other side, as I
thought. But something in the surroundings
looked familiar, and, on closer examination, I
discovered to my very great surprise that I had
come out within a few yards of where I had
entered. I had made a circle to the left. I tackled
it again—this time taking care to walk to the
right of any tree directly in front—and came out
all right. ... , . ,
On mentioning this incident to another neigh-
bor, who was a keen observer of the bush and its
peculiarities, he told me that had I noticed the
‘lean ’ of the timber I might go through at any
time, day or night, without making a mistake.
Asked to explain, he declared that a big majority
of the trees leaned towards the north-east ; and in
confirmation of his theory, he on many occasions
left my place for his home, on the darkest nights
and at any hour, and would go straight through
without turning a hair —merely standing by a tree
occasionally, and looking skywards to see which
way ‘ she cast.’ _ _ .
Have other bushmen noticed this peculiarity of
the forests of the interior? and what is the
reason ?
I had another somewhat similar experience a
few years earlier —weather conditions correspond-
ing exactly. (Here let me remark that the man
who gets lost while the sun is in full view is only
a johnny-come-yesterday bushinan). I was travel-
ling on horseback from the Yanko, and attempt-
ing to make a bee-line for my home in the Murray
Hut district. I succeeded very well until reach-
ing a gate on a fence four miles from my selection.
The rest should have been plain sailing, After a
time buildings appeared in front —they were old
buildings; mine were new. I bad struck up against
an out-station of Coree, nine miles east of niy
destination. I had gone that distance to the left,
and on horseback. . ;
Now, the theory that my right leg is longer
than the left couldn’t very well apply to my horse
also. Besides, my tailor assures me that my left
leg is quite an inch longer than its fellow. So
what say the pundits?—K.D.
Whilst running up a horse in January, ’BO, my
mount became unmanageable, bolted, and threw
me. Dazed by the fall, I ran after him bare-
headed for a considerable distance. Finally he
disappeared in the scrub, and I returned to get
my hat. About an houFs fruitless search in a
semi-conscious state convinced me I was hatless
and lost. This was about 6 a.m. From then
until about noon I vainly endeavored by coQ-eeittg
to attract attention ; and then T tried to make for
a fence I remembered passing (I had sense enough
to tear a piece off my shirt and tie it on my head).
At 5 p.m. Sergt. Campbell, the well-known Q.
tracker, with another bushtnan, ran me down some
17 miles from where I was thrown : but as I had
diverged to the right a good deal I must have done
over 20 miles. I knew nothing of what I had
done sinoe about noon; and had tak<'noff my shirt
and boots. The mosquitoes, sand-flies, and heat
seemed terrible for a while ; then I grew light-
headed, and the rest was a blank. —V.


Medical.
DR. FREEMAN.
THAT THIS NOTED SPECIALIST
Stands pre-eminently at the bead of the medical

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A FALLING STAR.
Against the purple arch of night,
Red glows the fiery shield of Mars,
And overhead the Milky Way
Shines faintly with its bridge of stars.
The mountain-peak that guards the west
Looms blackly thro’ the shadows ; now
A crescent moon has climbed the height—
A diamond on a swarthy brow.
Hand clasped in hand are lovers twain ;
Against his breast her fair head lies;
And in the east a falling star
Leaves trails of glory in the skies.
“ A falling star,” she cries. “ A soul
Gone forth on its last flight alone.”
Nor dreams the star falls for a life
That once had closely touched her own.
Hereafter she will hear that he
Called forth her name with hia last breath,
And marvel that no echo came
To dim her joy with thoughts of death.
First love ! last love ! the last is best.
First love —dead ashes of the past;
Who waits to dream of the first kiss
When lips are thrilling with the last ?
Kiss—aye, and vow ! The starry worlds
For centuries have viewed the same.
Such lips, such breasts, such wealth of hair,
But dust to dust, from whence they came !
Kiss—aye, and vow ! A sweetness lies
In each new flower that seeks the sun;
Tho* near at hand the dead husk lies
That once was just as fair a one.
Love rules the world, and every gate
(Save Death’s) to its bright key unbars,
Since parted lovers built across
The midnight sky—the “ bridge of stars.”
Q.
Ethel Mills.
THE THEORY OF THE THING.
Love is a thing too beautiful for use,
That should be kept apart from toiling life ;
A dawning hope, a theory abstruse,
Not to be touched by workday haste and strife.
Love is so strange, so exquisite and pure :
An evil thought can stain her gleaming crest;
And yet she will resist, and will endure
The flames of hell for him whom she has blessed.
On him she squanders all her wondrous gifts,
The total happiness of her embrace :
He dreams of her, he hopes, he yearns—and drifts
To death at last—and never saw her face !
Q.
VON KOTZR.
GOOD LUCK.
Good Luck is the coyest of all coy birds.
She cares no jot for the nets we spread ;
She lifts her wings at our flatt’ring words,
And pipes a song in the deeps o’erhead.
The more we follow she speeds the more ;
She recks but little of sob or sigh ;
But should we rest, when the chase is o’er,
She droops her wings and she ventures nigh ;
Ventures nigh with the sweetest strain
That ever rang in a mortal’s ear ;
But wouldst thou seize her —she soars again
To distant blue on her pinion dear.
Thou wilt not hold her in marble hall ;
Thou canst not cage her in lowly cot;
And that man she loveth the best of all
Who hath scorned her gifts and her name forgot.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
FROM A GARDEN.
You violets, my love’s delight,
Go, tell him that I think on him
All through the night (though moonlit, dim
Unless he comes), and dawning light
Is fair with him. Sweet mignonette,
Remind my love that all the sweet
Brought to ine by his coming feet
Goes also when he goes. Forget,
O pansies dear, forget not this
My message, that my dreams are fair
With his sweet eyes, his twining hair,
His tender touch, his rose-red kiss.
And thou, the image true of love,
Dear rose, say that my heart is true,
That he is dearer still than you,
And that my love shall constant prove.
S.A.
LALAGR.
THE STOCKMEN OF THE WATERS.
When the Darling chokes in her rain-fed rage
With a breast-foam flecked with pride,
She leaps the banks as she whirls to wage
Fierce war with the countryside;
Ho ! the great trees crash and the waters splash,
And the grim logs gird and groan
In the flecking froth, whilst the river’s wrath
Breaks loud in a raucous moan.
The stockmen spread on the faint, far tracks
Ere night on the ranges nears,
And silence wakes to the ringing cracks
That gather the browsing steers ;
For the torrents fly where the land was dry
To dust in the morning’s ride,
And swiftly run where the noon’s bright sun
Shone over the wheatfields wide.
Ho ! the ride, the ride, with a curdling hymn
Of death in the waters’ cries !
The plunge, the splash, and the strenuous swim
Where the flooded lowland lies !
For the mad flood’s roar is a call to war,
And woe to the laggard beast,
For a life is told if it may not hold
To the first foot-hills at least.
The cattle are rolling red-rimmed eyes
At the crash of a falling tree ;
A heifer sharpens the din with cries
For the calf she cannot see ;
But the course is done, for their feet have won
The turf of the climbing lands,
And the girths are freed as a stockman’s steed
Wide-legged and shivering stands.
Vic.
H. A. CONANT.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate dny
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles ,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing ivith Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
ilength, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
•communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
•desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
•other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
•and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
•interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Wcst.ralia and, Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Diving to the imposition of l\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage— ljd. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 4, 1897.
The Second Federal Convention.
The Federal Convention resumes business
this week with very moderate prospects.
During the last four months the cause of
Australian nationhood has gone through
many vicissitudes. At the very beginning
of the recess all the Premiers deserted it
and went to the land of Buhl to jubilate,
and get indigestion, and seize upon any title
that chanced to be passing, and this unanim-
ous abandonment by the alleged leaders of
the movement materially diminished public
interest in the matter. Of the under-
lings who were left in command, only
Holder, of South Australia, and Isaacs, of
Victoria, produced even moderately good
results with the Convention Bill. Horace
Tozer made a pretence of bringing Queens-
land into the movement, and then clumsily
shuffled back into his old local groove. The
Westralian Parliament never met at all until
a few days ago ; the wild-cat province is in
desperate straits now to keep its boom going,
and Acting-Premier Wittenoom is a fifth-
rate man, who could hardly be trusted with
a Parliament lest he should say foolish things
to it about the financial stinks of the Govern-
ment, therefore it was considered best to
shut up the show in Forrest’s absence.
Under these circumstances the West-
tralian Legislature has hardly even pre-
tended to consider the Federation question.
The Tasmanian Legislature did no good to
it. The South Australian and Victorian
Assemblies made one or two democratic
amendments, and the latter addetl on a
miserably unworkable alleged Referendum,
but, on the whole, they left things very
much as they were, and the draft Constitu-
tion, when they were finished with it, was,
on the whole, no better for their interfer-
ence. And the New South Wales Legisla-
ture knocked every important feature out of
it, and deprived the proposed Common-
wealth Parliament of all semblance of
authority, and left things a sh apeless Wreck.
* * % *
The Commonwealth Bill, When it left the
Adelaide Convention, was A wretchedly Ith-
perfect measure. It didn’t provide for the
Federal control of the existing railways or of
future railway Construction, the consolida-
tion of the public debts, the restriction of
future provincial borrowings, or the Federal
control of rivers ; it gave the Central
Government nothing like the definite
superiority over the merely provincial
authorities which it should have ; and it
made a half-hearted and unworkable com-
promise on the financial question—a com-
promise which was so uncertain that 'it gave
no real satisfaction to anybody. Yet The
Bulletin feels inclined to withdraw a 'good
many of the unkind things which it
said about the Convention. Of the five
provincial Legislatures which have hacked
and haggled more or less at its work, the
best that can be said for four of them is that
they did very little damage. Not one of
them made the scheme any better or any
more intelligible. Not one had sufficient
national sentiment to propose enlarging the
powers of the Commonwealth and making it
a real Government, instead of a thin shadow.
Not one advanced the statesmanlike proposi-
tion that the railways should be consolidated,
so as to make one national system instead
of a series of incongruous fragments, or
offered any proposition for the consolidation
of the provincial debts. Four of them left
the unsatisfactory financial arrangement prac-
tically as it was, and offered fio amendment
worth mentioning; the fifth (New South
Wales) struck it out, and, not being able to
devise anything even as good, left its place
blank. Compared to the ineptitude and
parochialism of the provincial Legislatures,
the Convention was a marvellously wise insti-
tution. and its scheme, by comparison with
most of the amendments made upon it, Was
a brilliant piece of statesmanship.
« « * *
The draft Constitution was submitted to
the Legislatures in order that their aggregate
wisdom might devise improvements upon it.
The principal improvements which they send
along are as follows :—New South Wales de-
mands peremptorily that Sydney shall be the
capital of the Federation. It proposes to
abolish the Federal Court of Appeal, and
leave things as they are. It proposes to
leave out the Inter-State Railway Commis-
sion which was to regulate railway freights
on the borders and put an end to the ruinous
war of rates which is estimated as costing
the country £300,000 per annum, and to
leave things as they are. It proposes to
abolish the Federal control of the Murray
and leave things as they are ; it requires
very little wisdom to propose that things
should remain as they are, and most of the
New South Wales amendments amount to
nothing more than that. It refuses to assent
to the idea of equal representation in the
Federal Senate—the only condition on which
the smaller States agreed to come in ; it
objects to all the financial proposals without
offering anything in their stead, and it wants
the “recognition of Goo.” Victoria asks
for very little except One-man-one-vote in
the Federal elections; the “recognition of
Goo ” ; and a Referendum scheme to avert
deadlocks, which scheme is so exceedingly
careful not to coerce even the smallest
minority that it could hardly remedy one
deadlock in a century. South Australia
wants One-man-one-vote, and demands that
the Federal control of the Murray, which
New South Wales repudiates, shall extend
to all the tributaries of that river. The
other two provinces, so far, have proved
themselves reasonable, so far as details are
concerned, and ask for nothing in particular.
* * * *
Taking everything into consideration, the
Convention starts its second meeting under
worse auspices than it did its first. Last
time a deadlock between the small States
(South Australia, Tasmania, and Westralia),
which asked for an altogether absurd share
of power, and the two large ones (New South
Wales and Victoria) was only averted by
the departure of the ten Westralian dele
gares who were called away to fight an election
in their own country. This left the parties
even, and a compromise was arrived at. This
time it is the Victorians who will be called
away, towards the end of the proceedings,
to fight an election, and this will leave the
30 delegates of the small states to run riot
and demand all manner of impossibilities,
with only the ten Cornstalk members to re-
present the other side of the question. The
delegates meet this time with the knowledge
that their respective Parliaments have given
them none of the assistance they expected,
and care comparatively little about the
matter. Some of the proposed amendments
are obviously intended to make any kind of
Federation useless or impossible ; the others,
except the utterly unimportant “recognition
of God,” are mostly calculated to mike the
provincial disagreements worse than they
were before. And the recognition of God
looks bad among the other amendments,
which are mostly evidences of parochial
brute-selfishness, envy, greed and all manner
of uncharitableness.
♦ • * *
As regards the personnel of the Convention
it has probably deteriorated. The Tasmanian
contingent is apparently far less friendly to
the movement than it was. Carruthers
and Lyne, of New South Wales, labored so
hard to take every vestige of usefulness out
of the Convention Bill, and to deprive the
Commonwealth of every semblance of dignity
and authority that they can hardly be re-
garded as Federalists any longer. Brunker’s
heavy sj'ie’echlessness gives little clue to his
teal feelings, but his underhand conduct in
suppressing the late Mr. Eddy’s report in
favor of railway Federation stamps him also
as having turned Provincialist —if he ever
was anything else. Both the South Aus-
tralian and New South Wales Ministerial
contingents go back with a knowledge that
their Parliamentary followers are iri the
main stupidly indifferent; in both provinces
it was hardly possible eVeii to collect a
quorum t 6 discuss the measure. Not one
Parliament 6f the lot, save Victoria, gave its
delegation what could be called an enthu-
siastic ihandate to go ahead and do its best
for the cause of Federation, and Victoria
was on the extreme verge of a general elec-
tion—a circumstance which makes it neces-
sary for members to strike a note of high-
toned patriotism because the man who isn’t
patriotic in the last Session of a dying Par-
liament loses his seat. Where no election
was pending it Was only by a supreibe effort
that the Legislature did hot fall down dead
on top of the Unfinished measure.
* * * *
~ One thing is certain ; in New South Wales,
Victoria, South Australia!, and Tasmania the
people, by electing Federatiohists only and
leaving the few Provincial candidates in
a miserable minority, gave an almost
unanimous vote in favor of Australian
unity. And if the Convention delegates
are true to their trust they will follow
the mandate of the people whatever the
five Parliaments may say to the contrary.
In this matter the Parliaments do not repre-
sent the people ; the creation of a supreme
central authority will reduce the rank-and-
file of provincial legislators to even worse
insignificance than they haVe already at-
tained, therefore their interests and those of
the nation are absolutely at variance. The
duty of the Convention delegates is to make
the best Federal Constitution they are
capable of, even if in so doing they reject
every amendment suggested by every Parlia-
ment in Australia. The fact that almost all
the amendments tend to make the Federal
power weaker is no reason Why the Conven-
tion should not make it Stronger if its con-
victions lie that Way-. Even the fact that
not one Provincial Legislature favors Federal
Control of the railways and the public debts
makes it not one whit less the duty of the
Convention to effect that consolidation if it
feels that it should be effected. It owes no
duty or responsibility to Parliament ; every
delegate from the four Eastern provinces
holds his commission front ail entire pro-
vince ; the men who criticise their doings in
Parliament represent, on an average, one-
fiftieth part of a province each. Moreover,
the Convention delegates have a direct man-
date from the people on this one question ;
their parliamentary critics were appointed
on the fiscal issue, and the roads and bridges,
and local option and land reform issues, and
on all manner of other irrelevant subjects.
Therefore the Convention is fully justified
in refusing to be guided in any way by the
parochial tenor of the amendments laid
before it. In fact, the best hope for Federa-
tion now seems to lie in tying a large boulder
to all these amendments and dropping them
off Circular Quay into 60ft. of oblivion.
The Latest Thing- in Loans.
The Westralian Government has rushed
through a Bill authorising the raising of
£3,000,000 by the issue of Treasury bills, The
Forrest Government lias already floated
£2,100,000 of its securities on the public this
year, in addition to considerable sums obtained
from the banks on Treasury bills at the very
steep rate of 4 per cent. The first loan, issued
at the beginning of the year, went off with great
difficulty at a low price ; the second one, in May,
went off with still greater difficulty at a lower
price. In each case the public tendered for
only a portion of the amount asked for, instead
of subscribing it several times over as is
generally the case, and if a syndicate had nob
come to the rescue in one case and a bank in the
other, Westralia would have been a boom pro-
vince in very great difficulties. When the loan
money was exhausted it was very clearly inti-
mated that the British market wasn’t taking
any more Westralian securities at any
price, and £480,000 was raised on Trea-
sury bills at 4 per cent. It is also reported
that further sums were temporarily borrowed
at 4| per cent. When the Treasury bill money
ran short, about £BOO,OOO was lent from revenue
to keep public works going, and the Govern-
ment got down so low that it had only £97,000
left to meet all demands. More than one-fourth
of the new £3,000,000 is, therefore, already
spent, and if by any chance it should fail to
arrive after all the situation would be highly
interesting.
* » * *
Financial authorities in London still report
that any attempt to float another Westralian
loan in the usual fashion is hopeless ; the credit
of the land of red dust and defunct cat is at too
low an ebb. And, as money must he raised or
Westralia will make a burst compared to which
even the Victorian collapse of 1893 will he as
nothing, the proposal to raise £3,000,000 on
Treasury bills is about the only alternative.
These bear interest at 4 or 4| per cent., and are
a frightfully expensive expedient ; but, even if
it is necessary to put the interest up to 5 per
cent., which is possible, the money must be
raised. Where it will be raised is another
question, for the banks on which the last batch
of Treasury hills was unloaded are reported to
be not at all anxious for any more of these
ancient and fish-like securities, but presumably
if the interest is high enough somebody will
take them. Somebody or other will gener-
ally take almost anything if the interest is
only high enough. If the transaction comes off
Westralia will have added nearly £6,000,000 to
its debt in a year or fifteen months, which is
equal to £4O per head. In other words, it will
have borrowed almost as much per head of
population in 12 or 15 months as Victoria has
done in the whole period of its existence, ahd
it will have the biggest debt per inhabitant io
the seven provinces, bar that of Queensland
only !
i * t *
The new loan, provided nothing dreadful
happens to prevent there being any new loan at
all, will keep Westralia going at the present
rate till, perhaps, next June—possibly not
quite so long. Then another big loah Will be
needful if the collapse is to be staged off ally
longer. Yet no country catt go on borrowing at
the rate of £3O or £35 pbr head anhUallv fob
any length of time, and all the banks of "Aus-
tralasia cah’t absorb enough I’t'ea'siiry to
keep the boom afloat, ft is quite possible
that the present is tfie last Vnagiiificeiit borrow
that Westralia Will be able 'to do for a number
Of year'sif it is the second last one of the pre-
sent boom, that is the best that the countity
can hope for. The size of the collapse, which
must ensue, unless about, the richest geld
discovery on earth arrives at the right
moment to give the province a fresh
start, may be roughly estimated as follows :
Victoria built the biggest boom in the
modern records of Australia on an influx
of borrowed money which never exceeded
£4 per head per annum, and it made the worst
collapse in the modern records of Australia
because of the stoppage of that annual influx
of £4 per head. The Westralian Government
is trying to run its boom on about seven times
as large ail annual borrowing, and When the
bottom falls out of its'credit thebe Will, Unless
some unexpected Streak Of lUek ihtebvfen'es, Life
just about seven times 4a sbddeh and violent a
stoppage. Where Victoria fell 60 yards Wes-
tbalia has a quarter of a mile to fall ; that is
all the difference. The only redeeming feature
is that the Westralian boom lias been compara-
tively short, and the Westralian people haven’t
had time to forget how to live without booming!
* * * *
When the great slump cable itt Victoria this
public revenue fell One-thirdi Whfen the hatch
greater slump conies in Wfestralia thte revienil'e
Will probably fall chie-half-, or fiiore. Possibly
it iriay gd dovvii as hutch as two-thirds ;at pre-
stent it has been boomed up, in the midst of arti-
ficial prosperity, to just about three times the
average of the other six provinces, and when
the country gets down to living on its own re-
sources there is no visible reason to expect that
it will produce a much larger revenue per head
than the other Australias. It is ho llioi e fertile
than the average; it has hd htorte Ilian the
average number of iildlistriteS td fall back upon ;
and the bulk df the gold-yield from its handful
of rich mines, which should be a powerful sup-
port in time of emergency, goes abroad to pay

or in England. There is no gold-booth in

in the output ; alid, if the present vastly-

Westralia, acre for acre, Would he much less
of a gold-country than Victoria or Tasmania is
now, and the present position Of these two pro-
vinces is a fair test of the chalices of another
gold-boom in the West, now that the great
original insanity is over. The Westraliah
frenzy for the last two years lias been a bor-
rowed-money-boom pure and simple, but, even
with all the energy that, has been put into the

The revenue for July, ’97, was £12,000 less

* » * *
One solid reason why the Westralian revenue
must come down with a gruesome crash when
loans stop short, is to be found in the miserably
small production of the country in proportion
to revenue. In 1895, the last year for which
returns are available, New South Wales pro-
duced about £33.000,000 worth of wealth in all
shapes—meat,, wool, agricultural produce,
minerals, manufactured goods, and everything
else that could be turned into money, and the
Government took about millions of this, or,
roughly speaking, ss. lOd. in the £. In Vic-
toria the Government took about 4s. 3d. in the
£; in Queensland ss. in the £ ; in Westralia it
gathered in no less than 14s. in the £. At
least this is how it would be if the revenue
came out of the country’s earnings. But as the
Westralian who earns 40s. per week certainly
can't pay 28s. of that in taxes, and as the one
who earns £5 a week would probably throw all
the Six Families overboard before he would pay
£3 10s. in taxation and live on the other 305.,
the natural inference is that the bulk
of the revenue comes indirectly out of
borrowings. It comes in the shape of duties on
imported goods which are paid for out of bor-
rowed money. When the loan moneys stop a
vast proportion of the imports stop. When
they stop a very large proportion of the customs
revenue does likewise. When the loan moneys
cense the citizen who produces £5 worth of
wealtli per week must pay £3 10s. of it to the
Government if the revenue is to keep "up.* And
he won’t. It will take the most artistic system
of taxation to screw half that amount out of
him, and even in that case the State income
will go down 50 per cent. Anyone who can
picture an anguished fat man with an eye-glass
trying to make ends meet in N.S.W. on
£4,800,000 a year instead of £9.600,000 will
understand the Westralian position.

The Suffield Enquiry.
Sydney Freeman’s Journal, in wliat looks
like an inspired paragraph, proclaims that ex-
Minister for Justice O’Connor had practically
no option but to deny Suffield an enquiry
into his case,
U must be confessed that Suffielii’s present duress is
iirnhablv as much due to viol nt friends as to a mistaken
v These friends so exploited (he case in the public
l at ati early stage as to leave the then Minister for
■inslice Mr R. B. O’ConSoM, no option but to ask for new
jii ate nil eviderice a§ a pitt.iiiiiiary to reconsideration of
the ease.
\Vliat ibis ineans, if it means anything, is that
Mr. d’Co'NNOR got Ids back Up because SuF-
field’s friends grew tired of tjie delays and
circumlocutions o'f the Alleged Justices De-
partment, and exposed the matter in the
press. And if the paragraph does mean this,
the meaning is hone too creditable to Mr.
O'Connor ; since there is an implication that
he refused to do justice to Suffield because
Suffield’s friends transgressed routine. It is,
by the way, Mr. O’Connor’s particularly tough
and not at all intelligent minute on the case
which has done so much to block enquiry all
along. Tom Slattery, following him in office,
does not appear to have ever got farther into
the case than this minute ; and Minister Gould
has used it time after time as an excuse for not
yielding to his own “strong impressions”
received from Suffield’s representations. Mr.
O’Connor minutes, inter aria, that “ the sug-
gestion that the injuries prosecutrix received
were caused by the attack of a hull will not
bear looking into ’’—which is a very steep pro-
nouncement in view of the fact that five
minutes after the event prosecutrix herself,
when perfectly conscious, attributed her con-
dition to the bull (tnind you, The Bulletin is
not arguing that there was not a man, Home
man, in the case as well as the hull), and her
employer took it for granted the hull was to
blame until next morning, when the man (that
is to say a man) was mentioned. Perhaps,
however, the following important ' evidence was
omitted from the copy of the Judge's notes laid
before Mr. O’Connor —just as it was omitted
from the copy of the Judge’s notes laid on the
Legislative Assembly table
Croudace, Senior, Cross-Examined.
By Mr. Edmunds : The bull has since attacked a married
woman, and had broken some of her ribs. The bull had
since been destroyed. .
His Honor : I think, Mr. Edmunds, that it is of no use
trying to make out that the girl’s injuries were caused by
the bull. Tlie story of the girl is undoubted.. [His Honor,
of course, was Judge Windeykr.]
Mr. Edmunds : I think, your Honor, that, her statement,
about the bull is important, and I wish to lav stress on
the bull, as it will tend to show the state of mind the girl
was in that evening. .
His Honor : Oh, of course, you are entitled to get out
any evidence you please regarding her mind. She, how-
ever, denies using the words.
By Mr. Edmunds : Just before the hull horned the
woman, he had been illtreated by four men. When a hull
became excited be usually tore up the ground, and, know-
ing that, the grass around the spot where the girl was
found was earefullv searched, but no trace of the animal
could be found. The spot where the girl was lying was
about 70 or SO yards from the house, and was concealed
by shrubs. —Newcastle Morning Herald report of the
trial.
If Mr. O’Connor had not the benefit of this
cross-examination, his minute was, to some ex-
tent, excusable. But it was not excusable in
him to use Section 383 of the Criminal Law
Amendment Act —a section expressly designed
by the Legislature to meet such cases as the
Suffield case—to justify the strained position
he took up. That section declares that —
Whenever, after the conviction of a prisoner, any doubt
or question arises as to his guilt, or any mitigating cir-
cumstance in the case, or any portion of the evidence
therein, it shall be lawful for any justice, by direction of
the Governor —on the petition of the prisoner, or some
person on his behalf, representing such doubt or question
—or by direction of a Judge of the Supreme Court, of
his own motion—to summon and examine on oath all
persons likely to give material information on the matter
suggested.
There is certainly a grave “ doubt or question
as to Suffield’s guilt; and any intelligent
Minister, or any honest Minister, who did not
dread the malice of the police department or
the opposition of the Windeykr faction, would
long ago have ordered enquiry. Yet Minister
O’Connor minuted that, applying this plain
section to that dubious case, it was impossible
for him to order an enquiry unless “ fresh
material evidence” was produced. Fresh
material evidence was produced, in the four
missing affidavits ; but the point is that the
relevant section says not a word of “ fresh evi-
dence ” —which is not law, hut O’Connor : irri-
tated by press advocacy ?

Cook’s Local Option Bill.
Postmaster-General Cook’s Local Option Bill
appeared in the N.S.W. Assembly just before
the adjournment, and it will probably be heard
of again when Parliament meets after the
Federal Convention. Therefore the present is
as good a time as any for saying something
severe about that ill-judged measure. Cook’s
Bill, like other Local Option measures, is a mix-
ture of injustice, blatant hypocrisy, cowardice,
feeble time-serving, and other sins, and there is
no honesty in it. It proposes, in the first
place, that the vote of a majority shall decide
whether the number of public-houses in any
district shall remain as it is, or be reduced, or
whether public - houses shall be abolished
altogether; hut there is no provision
whereby the majority may decide that the
number of public-houses shall he increased.
Of the four issues involved it is only pro-
posed to submit three to the electors, which
is a mean and futile way of smothering, or
attempting to smother, public opinion. And
P.M.O. Cook so far lacks the courage of his
opinions that he dare not submit even the whole
of his own case to the popular vote. His Bill
deals with the selling of drink by retail only,
whereas it should deal with drink in all its
aspects. Otherwise Postmaster Cook is only
a knock-kneed and apologetic brother weakly
pandering to the rich wholesale crog-person
If the Albury district, for instance, goes in for
Prohibition that vote should not only shut up
the bars where beer is supplied to the local
resident, but it should absolutely close the local
wine-making industry also. Every vineyard
where grapes are grown for wine-making pur-
poses should be destroyed, and the famous
local wines should disappear out of the market.
Supposing the district, decides, in due form,
that drink is a curse, then that decision
should carry with it the obligation to cease
making, selling, exporting, or in any way pro-
fiting by the objectionable thing. Albury
should even give up being a distributing centre
for drinking districts in the neighborhood ; if
it is solid on the fact that drink shouldn t exist,
then the bard drinkers of Mullengandra and
Howlobg and Walbundrie should import their
grog via some other totvn, and the wholesale
fherchant, importer, wine-maker* wine-exporter
alnd rfetdif publican should all be shut tip to-
gether. Similarly, if Sydney votes for prohibi-
tion, that act shohld close OvOry brewery in the
city and every wholesale dealfer, as Urell as
the miserable retailer. If other parts of
N.S. W. want to import liquor it should
come in via some other port; btit Syd-
ney, as a place which has sworn - off,
should have no connection with the matter. If
other parts of New South Wales want to ex-
port liquor, it should go out via some other
port. If there must be a wholesale drink-trade,
then merchants at unregenerate drinking cen-
tres should conduct it, and the Prohibition city
should keep itself clean, regardless of cost.
For Sydney to solemnly put its ban on alcohol,
and yet live, in a measure, by making beer to
lead outside brethren astray, importing beer
and whisky and exporting wine for the same
purpose, distributing drink, advertising drink,
and being the great wholesale drink centre of
New South Wales, would be loathsome hypo-
crisy. Even the papers in prohibition districts
should be compelled to cease publishing, for
filthy lucre, advertisements inciting the public
to buy and use alcohol. The Bulletin
doesn’t in the least object to Local Option,
if the majority wants it. It will get on with-
out whisky well enough, and if the majority
decides that it should get on without whisky it
is willing. But it asks that the issue
should be put fairly before the community, and
that the miserable Cold Tea crowd should be
decently honest and have the courage of its
opinions.
The Mixed Gauges.
The railway commissioners of New South
Wales, Victoria, and S.A. have been sitting in
conclave at Melbourne on the desirability of a
uniform railway-gauge. They have decided
that the diverse gauges are a nuisance and a
source of great loss, which everybody knew
already, and that it would probably be the
wisest thing to adopt the N. 8. W. 4ft. BJin.
gauge all round. Almost everybody was already
agreed on that also. And there the matter
stops short, and is likely to stop until that rail-
way federation, which short-sighted local poli-
ticians are doing their best to prevent, conies
along. If the railways were one concern under
one management it would simply be a matter
of making the necessary alteration and charg-
ing the expense to the Federal railway
department ; there would be no question of
allotting the cost among the different pro-
vinces. As it is the question is something like
this : If the N.S.W. gauge is adopted, then the
Vic. and S.A. lines and rolling-stock must be
altered for the sake of uniformity. As the
alteration is for the benefit of all three pro-
vinces, the cost must be borne equitably among
them. What is an equitable division ? Should
the bill be simply charged to the three pro-
vinces according to population ? Or should it
be charged according to the amount of railway
possessed by each province, or would it be more
equitable to take both matters into considera-
tion ; and, if so, how could it be done? Sup-
posiii"- the alterations are made in Vic. and
S.A., and the bill is £2,500,000, and N.S.W.’s
share of it is so much, how much should the
other provinces allow as discount in considera-
tion of the fact that the money is spent in their
territory and finds work for their population?
Should the three provinces stand the bill for
altering all the lines, or only the interprovincial
ones ? If so, what are the interprovincial lines ;
if the term is applied to the finished State-
owned lines connecting two provinces, it covers
the Sydney-Adelaide railway only ; if it covers
also the connecting lines which are still
unfinished but which will be main interprovin-
cial lines some day, there are about eight of
them to be considered. No one seriously
expects that the three provinces will come to
any settlement on these points, or on any one of
them. A Federal railway authority could
decide them easily, or rather it would not have
to decide them at all, but the three separate
authorities must do a painful and elaborate
division, and they have never been known to
come to a definite conclusion about anything.
As matters stand, discussions about a uniform
railway-gauge are about as useful as discussions
on the advisability of abolishing wine and
women.

Experto Crede!
Dear Bulletin,— As regards the supposed
responsibility of strong drink for crime, le i me
point out that enough account is not taken of
the fact that the drinking man is hardly e er
ill-affected towards his fellow-creatures, and
that whatever moral deterioration he may
undergo very seldom takes the direction of pre-
meditated offence against society. He may
trespass against the person under vinous
excitement, or against property under poverty-
pressure caused by drink ; but, for heartless
and cold-blooded wickedness, we have to look
mostly to the man who is “ temperate ’ merely
by the accident that his social and sympathetic
tendencies were never strong enough to expose
him to the risk of forming the alcohol habit in
the way in which, ninety-nine times out of a
hundred, it makes its insidious beginnings.
Alcohol may diminish temperamental equa-
bility and weaken the will, but—speak-
ing ' as the possessor, unfortunately, of
extended experience in the matter—l have
never known it materially to vitiate the origins’
moral equipment, arid I can see nothing but a
bad case of post hoc in the assumption that
liquor is an extensive cause of crime, in any
other sense than that it doubtless sometimes
eliminates the caution which is the only reason
why many men are not the criminals for which
nature otherwise intended them. The main-
spring of all such crimes, for instance, as
Butler’s —assuming that the perpetrators are
not literally insane—is simply a brutal and in-
exorable selfishness; they are the result, in
other words, of the anti-social instinct in its
highest development —a circumstance which
has a significant bearing on the frequently-
noted fact that the deliberate manslayer—like
the systematic seducer and the saintly whole-
sale swindler—is very rarely a tippler and very
often a teetotaller. Nor is there anything
more powerfully characteristic of the hypo-
critical worship of superficial “ respectability
than the fact that, with us, the most ment-
ally and morally despicable creature that
doesn’t happen to use alcohol considers itself
entitled by that solitary circumstance to
a smug superiority over the man who very
often possesses—to balance that single weak-
ness—pretty well every quality that goes
to validate humanity. That it is not the most
grossly-compounded natures which run most to
alcoholism is a matter beyond question and
since, with men as with women, there is no
virtue so vociferous as the virtue that means no
more than lack of temptation, it is easy to
understand how the only failing that cannot
long be successfully concealed is as convenient
foT self-rectification to the secret and more
sensual sinner as it is to the cheap sociological
prater for fotcihg an absurdly false deduction
into the teeth of common observation. Sar-
donyx.
The relation between agent and producer in
N.S.W. calls loudly for legislation. This rela-
tion is considerably elucidated by the digest of
the Registrar in Bankruptcy in a recent case:
“ The bankrupts spent all the capital they could raise,
and were only enabled to carry on by using the money
received from the sale of their constituents’ wool. They
paid into their general account ±4OlO 10s. 7d., which
they had received as agents only from 18 persons who had
consigned their wool to them for sale. To none of these
persons had they made any advance, nor had they any
lien whatever on the wool. They had expended these
moneys for their own benefit; as on the date of the assign-
ment'of their estate they had in their account, into which
they had paid the ±4OlO 10s. 7<L, only ±5 19s. sd. They
had spent it not only in carrying on their business, but
also in paying? their private debts. They were insolvent,
and knew' it, yet they went on as agents, paying their prin-
cipals’ money into their general account. After they knew
their case was hopeless, they paid at least ±32sointo then
account which money was in their hands as agents only.
They appropriated to their own use over £4OOO belonging
to their principals. There was no doubt it would have
been impossible for them to have carried on beyond the
first year if they had had a trust account. They said that
no woolbrokers kept a trust account. Their consignors
were chiefly small pastoralists, depending almost entirely
upon the produce of their wool to tide them over the next
12 months.” The Registrar refused the certificates of
discharge.
The Warren (N.S.W.) Woolgrowers’ Associa-
tion requested Mr. Macdonald, M.P. for
Coonamble, to move for the introduction of
legislation to cope with the agent-producer
grievance. This member asked the N. S.W.
Minister for Justice to draft a Bill of the nature
of the Maoriland Act, regulating the licensing
of auctioneers ; and to extend its principles to
agents in general. Clause 19 of the M.L. Act
reads :
Every auctioneer shall apply the proceeds of sale, when
realised, as follows (a) In or towards payment of ex-
penses, commission, and other charges of, or incidental
to, the sale, (b) In or towards payment of any moneys
owing to such auctioneer by the person on whose behalf
the property was sold, (c) The balance of such proceeds
shall be held by the auctioneer exclusively for such
person, to be paid to him or as lie may direct, and, until
so paid, such balance shall be paid into a bank carrying
on business under the authority of an Act of the Genera
Assembly, to a general or separate trust account, and
such balance shall not be available for payment of the
debts of any other creditor of such auctioneer, nor shall
such balance be liable to be attached, or taken in execu-
tion, under the order or process of any court, at the
instance of any such creditor.
The last stage of proceedings is the answer of
the Minister for Justice to Mr. Macdonald,
stating that he has given instructions for the
preparation of a draft bill on the subject for the
consideration of the Cabinet.
Almost every Australian who visits London
notes the magnificent body of police that keep
order in the city—their size, and their vast
chests, and their elegant uniforms, and the like ;
and comes back to drivel about them in the
local press, and ask why the Australian cop
isn’t like them. The passing Australian has a
wild idea that the beautiful order he sees round
the Bank of England exists all over the
city. The following extract from Lloyds
about the condition of Clerkenwell, an import-
ant London suburb, throws a new light on
things:—
The shooting of a little girl, Margaret Smith, in Cler-
kenwell, is the culminating horror of a state of terrorism
that is a disgrace to London itself. A correspondent
who has been over the ground says that Clerkenwell has
been at the mercy of a band of young desperadoes for
something like 12 months. One policeman was no good
against gangs of armed roughs. There is not one that is
not armed with some weapon. “ Our children—especially
our girls—run dangers that it makes one shudder to con-
template.” The fights began 12 months ago be-
tween the Islington, Chapel-street gangs and the lads
around here. At first they used to fight with their
fists, but last year they got sticks and fought with. Then
they took to using pokers and bars of iron. The next
things they' got were some pistols, with which they went
freely about, and recently they have got revolvers.”
It sounds just like Woolloomooloo. The next
returned Australian who wants to drivel to Tiie
Bulletin about the beautiful order maintained
in London, and to ask why the Australian
bobby doesn’t do likewise, will leave the office
on his head.
Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords sadly feels
the loss of its boss, Sargood, now jubi-
lating in foreign parts. Sargood knows how
to mutilate a Bill received from the As-
sembly, without wounding it so grievously
as to make it unrecognisable. At present,
the other 47 Rich Landlords simply hit all Bills
with a bludgeon—so that even ihe Argus utters
indirect reproofs. The Exported Products Bill
was thrown out in its entirety but when the
Government threatened to refrain entirely from
supervising and assisting exportation, there
was a wail from the Tory ranks that they
didn’t mean to throw the Bill out, and wouldn’t
do it again. Then a Bdl for consolidating the
mining laws was brought forward. The first
f.Vifnrt rko tonsils did was to knock out all the
vital clauses relating to accidents, royalties,,
ventilation, etc., some of the most important ol
which had been the law of the land for 20 years.
The Government immediately withdrew the
Bill, and it was seen at once that they had been
given a splendid cry with which to rouse the
mining communities at the approaching genera
elections. Once more the Argus crowd found*
itself in difficulties, and had to humiliate itselt
before the Ministerialists —and, at last advices,,
here the matter rested.
The publication of the late Commissioner Eddy’s-
report in Sydney Telegraph decides two-
things. First, there was a report; and.
second, it was definitely in favor of Federal
control of the railways. Mr. Eddy probably-
knew more about railway matters than any
other man in Australia, and his decided opinion-
in favor of handing over the whole business to-
the Federal Government counts for a great deal.
The GoD-fearing Ministry comes out of tins
business in its usual clumsy and uncouth*
fashion. At first, Brunker and co. professed'
not to know that there was any report. Then
they didn’t know what was in it. Then they
attempted to explain that they hadn’t got it,,
and couldn’t produce it. Attorney - General
Want even declared that Mr. Eddy was against
railway Federation, and used that statement to
bolster up the petty provincial views of his
own Little Cornstalk Party. It now appears
that the report was perfectly accessible
after all, so the inference is that the
temporary Brunker and co., being themselves
anti-Federalists, tried to suppress the document
which told so strongly against them, and
bungled miserably, as usual. That part of the
Government which has been running the show-
in Reid’s absence is utterly provincial and
parochial in sentiment, and has worked hard
all through to make Federation impossible.
Part of it, in the shape of Jack Want, did its
share by damning Federation openly. Car-
rutiiers enthused about l'ederation and strove
to take every shadow of power from the Com-
monwealth Government. And Brunker and
the rest gave no support and didn’t even pre-
tend to be interested in the subject. It is an
awful thing for a country to be run by a large,
dull, heavy-breathing, temporary man who is
only keeping the cigar alight for the fellow
who is away.
Concerning a S.A. electoral peculiarity :
The most serious allegation against Charleston, the
S.A. Leg. Councillor who resigned his membership of the
Labor-party and his seat, was that he had sold his side to
prolong his own tenure of office at ±2OO a year. In this
wav : Dr. Magarey, M.L.C., was last year on the brink of
resuming his seat, but eventually, against his doctors
advice, decided to hang on. Had he resigned, under the
peculiar S.A. arrangement, Charleston would have had
to risk return at the next general election instead of at
the election three years afterwards; and Magarey con-
fided to a Labor-member that lie had, in fact, decided to
hang on partly that he “ might not reduce the term of
office of his triend Mr. Charleston.” After this it, unfor-
tunately for Charleston, happened that he and Magarey
were members of a small select committee on the Hospital
dispute, and he pretty generally sided with the doctor
against the Govt, representative. Hence, in view of
Mag area’s confidence, the allegation of an understanding
between the pair, with the inference that Charleston
was repaving his debt to the doctor. For present pur-
poses it isn't, of any consequence that. Magarey denies
ever having spoken about the subject to Charleston ,
the ignominious squabble may be lost sight of m order to
observe the peculiar feature of the S.A. plan, by which
one member has it in his power to lengthen or reduce
another member’s term. That he has that power follows
from the fact that each member is elected nominally for
the full term ; the member sent in at a by-election isn t
sent in for the unexpired portion of the term of the man
whose place he takes. Charleston’s present resigna-
tion, for instance, will give somebody a full term,
while Fuller, who would otherwise not have to retire
until 1903, will go out in 1900. Theoretically something
is to be said in favor of the plan. A person is none the
less worthv of a full term because lie is elected in 189 b
instead of‘in 1897. Besides, the plan keeps the member-
ship trifle more up to date, since in practice very few
men can serve the full term of nine years or so. In prac-
tice however, by-elections arouse so little interest that it
is questionable whether the man returned at one of them
really is fairly entitled to enter upon the same footing as
a member who is returned at a general election when
interest is wider and deeper. The S.A. theory is partly
right, but (he practice breaks down, lor the people
won’t recognise that a by-election isn t a by-election in
the ordinary sense at all; and, in the second place,
physically incompetent members hang on in order to
oblige their impecunious friends.
The N.S.W. Leg. Council reduced the Federa-
tion Bill to such unrecognisable wreck that.
Barton refused at last to have any further con-
nection with it, and it left the Land and Income
Tax Validating Bill over till October, and
Generally played the intoxicated ox all through
the session. The Council is now just about as
bigoted and obstructive and obstinately Tory
as it was before Reid sent up his ten alleged
democrats to leaven the mass. No one hears
much of these ten now. They are not nearly
so enthusiastic as they were, for one thing ;
the Councillors are not paid, and men who have
their living to make have either to make it by
grinding axes inside the House, or else they have
to hustle for a crust outside while the same
little crowd of rich men run the show. Also
the ten are not so democratic as they were ; not
one man in a thousand can he an irrespon-
sible autocrat with a life billet and remain
democratic. The atmosphere of the place
has settled down upon them, and, barring
the fact that there, is a stronger Freetrade ele-
merit in the Council than there was, things
have hardly altered at all. The need for Upper
House reform is almost as urgent now as it was
at last general election. So long as the Council
represents nobody, and is responsible to nobody
for its actions, however had they may be, and
the members are unpaid so that only l icit men
can give their serious attention to public busi-
ness, the mere addition of poor, commonplace
James Blanksby and Benjamin Backhouse
and a few like them to the list makes
no difference worth mentioning. It has made
so little difference, in fact, that the pub-
lic, if it was put <m its solemn oath, couldn t
tell for certain whether the first-mentioned
person wax Blanksby or only plain Blanky.
Acting-Premier Horace Tozer’s very latest
reason why Queensland should stand out of the
Federal movement is that he doesn't feel sure Par-
liament would pass a bill for the election of ten
Convention delegates by the people. , If he was
sure, Tozer, it seems, would go ahead with
enthusiasm in his soul. Considering that the
Tozer crowd has twice come to grief over a bill for
preventing the people having any voice in the
election, this sudden outbreak of Democratic
principle comes a little late.


Short of compulsory cannibalism, seldom
has there been a more harrowing sea-tale that!
that of the crew of the Norwegian barque; Sela-
don, who, uport provisions for about three days;
starved it out for 30 ; jammed with their dyirig
captain into one boat —after the capsizing of. the
other—like sardines in a tih : disappointed of
island after island ; roasted by day artd frozen by
night; tortured with thirst and hunger, but
manly, patient; and uncomplaining, till, at the very
Utmost extremity, they made land—too far gone
to do aught but let the surf cast them ashore.
And, through all this hell upon earth, not the
semblance of a dispute—not a hint at the most
horrible of all resources. Olsen and Andersen and
Nansen—pray that they may be in the boat with
you when you ate cast away at sea.
It appears to be now certain that the
Turner Government will be reconstructed after
the October elections. Williams, who has been a
ghastly failure in the Railways; is to he retired.
Duffy, who has not even kept the Post-office
respectable; will resign. In the event of Graham
Berry not returning to the Speaketship, Duffy
will offer himself, ahd will not be accepted.
MasOn is said to have a lien on the billet.
Taverner is to be taken away from the Public
Works Department, but will be given an-
other portfolio. It is said by many that
Best, one of the least valuable of the
Ministers, will not be returned again by Fitzroy.
At any rate, it is not expected that he will con-
tinue in charge of two so important departments
as the Lands and Customs. To sum up, there
will be at least two portfolios to allot for next
session. One of these will be given to Trenwith,
and Alan M'Lean has a claim on the other.
M'Lean is now very strong in the House. If
there is a third vacant office (as is likely) it will
probably be offered to Higgins, perhaps the most
brainy thorough radical in Vic.

The seemingly dead and gone luck of
Sydney Royal woke up suddenly on Saturday
night, when the burlesque “ Trilby ” was raised
from among the immoral and grimy dead. The
■said luck has kept reasonably wide awake ever
since. There is a certain mesmeric quality about
Maggie Moore’s “ Trilby,” and about the earthy,
"clawsome Svengali and his familiar, Gecko, which
makes this little piece go, though on analysis
•there is nothing tangible in the thing itself. It is
so small that it is only by intense dragging at
■both ends that it can be made to spread over two
‘hours and a few minutes. There is amazingly
lit le dialogue; long spaces are filled in with the
mesmeric waving of Svengali’s talons among the
shadows in the background, and the shaking of
•alluvial deposit out of his mane, and the dreaming
■of “ Trilby ” down by the footlights, and the
shadow cast against the scenery by Gecko’s head,
which looks like a saltbush with a crow sitting on
top of it, out on the plains. The Bulletin hasn’t
yet begun to weary of Roberts’s Svengali. The
unaffected dirtiness of that mouldy man of music
is interesting, partly because Svengali is almost
the only man who ever did look frankly, un-
affectedly dirty on the stage. Anyone can see
'that his annual wash has been unavoid-
ably postponed, and his beard is fertile
with loam and decayed sundries. The writer
feels a desire to peg out a selection
on his more level parts, and to retire from the
tlaily grind and lead a peaceful agricultural exist-
ence on top of Svengali, and cultivate the surface
of that neglected person who makes music.
Maggie Moore and Walter Rivers (as Gecko) are
practically all the rest of the show, and these three
people get little assistance from the author, who
leaves them to rage very much according to their
own devices. Yet, barring a certain want of veri-
similitude about the bald and unconvincing piano
which Svengali rushes through every night, there
isn’t much to complain of in the result. The piece
revolves vigorously around nothing all the time,
and closes with a moderate-sized bang before the
audience has time to grow tired. It consists
mainly of melody and hair and shadows and other
irrelevaucies, and it is a success for no reason in
particular beyond the fact that Svengali obviously
requires to be disinfected and dry-blown. Yet it
seems to be a reliable success all the same.
