The “French Maid” will present herself
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday. Advance
reports state that she is a nicely-frocked damsel of
freat personal attractions, and that her golden
air isn’t suspended down her back more than is
absolutely necessary.
** * *
Sydney Lyceum has been leased by the
MacMahons—the Mighty Atom and Co. —and will
shortly break its long silence. The last thing in
the way of drama or comedy which attempted to
produce itself at this theatre was Cosgrove’s truly
•dreadful “ Squattocracy,” and on cold, wet nights
the curse which followed that play can still be
beard uttering itself on various parts of the
premises. The Bulletin’s —and everybody else’s
—friend, Alfred Dampier, will open the proceed-
ings shortly, and considering that there is
nothing in Sydney just now but burlesque and
variety business, the old “legitimate” should be
a pleasant change.
** m *
Harry Rickards’ venture at the Palace has,
so far, done well. The Biograph is mainly re-
sponsible for this. Now that that wonder has
settled down to steady work, the fact that it is
something really remarkable become more appa-
rent. It is a smooth-flowing illusion ; when it
presents Niagara to the public.gaze that cataract
hasn’t got the drunken jumps that have character-
ised some cataracts, and there are no shapeless
black blotches rushing unexpectedly up the falling
waters. “Jumbo” is admittedly the best and
most realistic fire-engine going, and the boat
which comes down the “shoot” is a spectacle
which is honestly calculated to make the public
hair stand up and balance itself with painful
accuracy on the scalp. Rickards announces that
he is going to turn the Palace into a comedy and
burlesque theatre by-and-bye, and among other
beautiful things which are to come Australia’s
lost goddess, Pattie Browne, is promised in a few
months’ time. The Biograph will probably keep
Rickards out of the Benevolent Asylum in the
interim—at least The Bulletin hopes so. ’Arry
would never wear the pious and resigned aspect
which is the correct thing for an “inmate.”
** * *
The Selbinis, who created a bubble-repu-
tation on the treacherous bike at a previous visit,
are back at Sydney Tivoli, and conducting them-
selves as disrespectfully towards the law of gravity
as formerly. Also, the co. at the Royal Standard
is now in its 45th consecutive week, and is dragging
the corpse of the patriotic drama round with its
usual vigor. There may be a lack of the finer
artistic touches about the Standard dramas, but
there is no theatre in Sydney where the heroine
is towed by the ear through such awful persecu-
tion for the money, or the villain wades in gore
with more stolid premeditation. Anybody who
feels that way can simply swim in blood at the
Standard for threepence. And when they kill the
miscreant, toward- 11 p.m., they do kill him ;
when there is a good house, and the co. is feeling
well, it is prepared to bash the miscreant out of
all shape, and shovel his remains into the furnace,
without any extra charge.
** * *
There was some talk of Frank Lincoln ex-
tending his clever entertainment for another
week, but the rumor proved unfounded, and the
old lady with the whistling front tooth closed on
Saturday. Business was a good deal better
towards the end of the week ; nevertheless Lincoln
probably feels called upon to make some severe re-
marks through his nose about the way Sydney
treated him. Even when the business improved
it amounted to very little, and the Centenary Hall
isn’t the best place for a humorist, anyhow. The
high platform looks too like a pulpit, and there is
a dampness in the air left over from the Wesleyan
infant that was baptised the previous Sunday,
and the threepenny that drops in the plate leaves
a faint, tinkling echo on the premises all through
the week. A cheerful entertainment doesn’t get a
show in a building where the sinner is habitually
damned every Sabbath.
** * *
The idea of the wondrously successful
Chinese pageant held in Sydney last Saturday
emanated from Manager Manders, of the Diamond
Jubilee Carnival Committee. They approached
Quong Tart, Sun Johnson, and J. A. Philp, of the
Chinese newspaper, but these, knowing the disin-
clination of the Chinese merchants to take part in
public matters and the clan jealousies of the com-
moner Chows, did not regard the prospects of
success as great. However, the Kwong Yek
Wah Bo took the matter up, and after several
weeks of “ dead lift,” the Asiatic apathy was dis-
pelled, and then Yee King Lee, Quong Tart, and
other prominent Chinese merchants came into the
movement. Law, M.P. for Balmain, had mean-
while visited Bendigo, and made tentative
arrangements for the loan of the “ properties ”
necessary for the carnival. Almost at the last
moment, however, the Bendigo Chinamen de-
manded a guarantee of ,£3OOO before the dresses
should leave their town. This was arranged for
in a few hours by the Lin Yik Tong, a mer-
chants’ society with £OOO,OOO or £BOO,OOO of trading
capital behind it, and the props, arrived in
Sydney, accompanied by a squad of property men.
The pageant, regarded purely as a spectacle, was
probably the finest ever held in Sydney. The
costumes were, for the most part, of silk, and the
accessories, from the palanquins and canopies
down to the weapons and the brocaded shoes, were
strictly de rigueur.
** * *
The last matinee of “ Sign of the Cross ”
occurs at the Princess’s to-day (Thursday), and
the ninth Melbourne week of that stately spec-
tacle will close to-morrow. Then Nero, and his
converted Prefect, together with Mercia, Berenis,
the opportune thunderstorm, and all the other
items in a remarkable success, will move along to
fresh fields of profit. The bill for Saturday (Sept.
4th) is “ Too Much Johnson,” a bright farcical
comedy by William Gillette, said to be the coming
Pinero of America. “Too Much Johnson,”
which has already been tested in Adelaide, will
re-introduce the Paulton-Stanley co. to Melbourne
notice.
** * *
George Rignold has struck Melbourne at
a favorable moment, and “ Lights o’ London ” is
doing such lively business at the Royal that the
season will be carried over several weeks longer
than the fortnight originally advertised. “In the
Ranks ” is to follow the tank drama ; also rumors
of a vigorous “Joseph of Canaan ” revival are in
the air. Meantime, “ The Lights ” holds the floor
every evening until further notice, and George
Rignold plays the hero as though he were deriving
much benefit from the cold plunge. One night
last week he sprained the sinews of his right arm
in a desperate struggle with the police, and next
night his rescue of Seth Preene from a watery
grave terminated in something like a drowning
match between the exhausted pair. Later on
George appeared with his arm in a sling, so when
it became necessary for him to wreak vengeance
upon the villain, Miss Hughes got over the diffi-
culty bysaying “Maimed as you are, thrash him
within an inch of his life.” Whereupon the
maimed one grabbed Diver with his left, and the
audience felt glad to find that an arm more or less

“ The Private Secretary ” is booming as of
yore, or rather more so, at Melb. Bijou, where the
second Uncle Cattermole improves on acquain-
tance, and gives an air of novelty to that strong
arrival from Calcutta. For anyone who saw
Frank Thornton’s show half-a-dozen times in the
years that have fled, the present Uncle is an in-
ducement to see it again. Miss Meta Pelham, as
the landlady, likewise deserves honorable mention
last week, and if the calamitous exponent
of the tailor character could only cut himself
from a new pattern, and become a different sort of
man entirely, the performance would be amusing
all the time. _As it is, the tailor leaves an occa-
sional impression of dull care hanging around the
proceedings until Frank Thornton comes to drive
it away. Melbourne is just now full of agricul-
turists who dwell in dark places where the foot-
fall of “The Private Secretary” has never yet
tinkled on the tufted turnip, so to speak, and as
that gifted and reverend bun-driveller gives place
to the “ Bookmaker ” on Saturday, these bucolic
characters are hereby warned to hurry up.

COLUMN
Victoria ended its financial year at 30th June
(rith a deficit of £196,098. Therefore the Seven Provinces
nan out a 9 follows ;
Victoria Deficit £196,098
New South Wales „ 170,945
Maoriland i Surplus 125,000
Tasmania » 47,732
Queensland ••• »•« » 0,000
Westralia » 3,298
South Australia ~ 2,140
Which is a considerable amount of deficit and not much
surplus. Still things are not quite so bad as they look.
Part of the N.S.W. expenditure is £150,000 paid off the
old deficit, so the total deficiency is only £20,945 more
than it was. Part of the Victorian shortage consists of
£250,000 paid off the old deficit, so the Vic. deficit is
really £53,902 less than it was. Allowing for these things
the figures would be as follows :
New South Wales Deficit £20,945
Maoriland Surplus 125,000
Victoria ... ... .< 33,902
Tasmania ... ... it 47,73 u
Queensland ... ... u 8,886
Westralia _ it 3,298
South Australia „ 2,140
The deficit above-mentioned is quite reliable,
earring the fact that it is probably more like £820,000
than £20,000, the paper shortage being kept down by
charging current expenditure to loans. The surpluses
are mostly fiction and financial Tozer, being also faked
by charging current expenditure to loans. Where the
loan expenditure is large it almost always covers a vast
number of items which should come out of revenue ;
where it is very small there is a fair chance that the
revenue accounts are nearly honest, simply for want of
opportunity to work so large a swindle ; but the only
time when the financial statements of the Seven Pro-
vinces will be wholly honest will be when there is no loan
expenditure at all. Last year Victoria spent £293,000 of
loan money; Queensland, 1,148,341; N.S. Wa1e5,£1,548,606.
Railways absorbed £193,000 of the Vic. loan expenditure,
and as it is impossible to work much financial swindle on
the remaining £lOO,OOO, Turner’s balance-sheet is cer-
tainly the most honest of the lot.
Turner estimates that next year, after paying off
another £250,000 of Shields deficiency bonds, he will end
with a shortage of £84,000. If this comes off, the accu-
mulated deficit will really be reduced by £166,000. By
recent conversion operations the Vic. Treasury saves
£19,741 per annum in interest, and it is promised that no
new loan will be floated this financial year (ending June,
’9B). The present Vic. balance-sheet is the first one since
1890 which has shown a reduction in the awful deficit,
and as Turner has an election on in about six weeks it
arrives verv opportunely.
• * • *
Westralia’s proposal to raise another .£3,000,000
loan vaguely suggests that someone is off his head. In
January last, W.A. issued £1,100,000 worth of stock in
payment for the Great Southern railway. In May, it
floated another £1,000,000 on general principles. It also
raised a considerable amount in Treasury bills, so the pre-
sent transaction makes £5,600,000 or more borrowed, or to
be borrowed, in one year. This a good deal more than
doubles Westralia’s debt in one act; until this year it was
only about £5.000,00n. In 1896 W.A. only borrowed
£750,000, and the same amount in 1895. These were
pretty large sums for about 130,000 people, and were
equal to an annual borrowing of £6,000,000 by N.S W. ;
still, they were moderate by comparison. In 1897 Wes-
tralia apparently resolved to borrow the entire earth if it
could be got.
• * » •
The West Australian Bank continues to do well.
The half-year’s profit to June 30t.h is £19,685, against
£16,703 and £12,284 for two previous half-years. The
usual 17)4 per cent, dividend absorbs £8750 ; £3OOO is
written off premises account; and the remaining £7935
is carried forward. Three years ago the half-year’s profit
of the W.A. Bank was £8387, but since then it has swelled
up continuously amid the howl and roar of the biggest
and maddest borrowed-money boom that even Australia
has ever witnessed. Still there are beginning to be signs,
despite the increase of profits, that the greatest part of the
boom is over. For two successive half-years deposits
have diminished, which is one symptom ; moreover, the
inevitable slump will strike the W.A. Bank harder than
some of its rivals, its business being confined to the great
boom province. One thing, however, is noticeable :it
only holds £20,000 of State securities, so the Forrest
Government has evidently not managed to load it up with
much of its recent issues of Treasury bills. And if the
new £3,000,000 issue of these bills actually floats, the load
will be so great that there will be serious possibilities of
ultimate default in W.A.; therefore, the W.A. Bank seems
to take a wise course in standing from under.
* * * •
The Land Mortgage Bank (Melb.) six months
ago persuaded its English creditors to reduce interest
on its £900,000 of debentures from 4J to per cent, for
five years, thus making a temporary saving of £4500 on
each half-year’s operations. With the strange cussedness
of dilapidated land banks in general it seems a lot worse
after the saving than before. This is how the L.M.B. has
been feeling according to its last seven reports :
June, ’94. Profit, £260 June, ’96. - Loss, £9696
Dec., ’94. Loss, 6038 Dec., ’96. ~ 10,494
June,’9s. „ 6617 June,’97. „ 14,425
Dec., ’95. „ 8847
In this last half-year the poor, sorrowful little bank was
■enjoying the full advantage of the reduced rate of
interest, and after saving £4500 in this direction it made
£3931 more of a loss than it did the half-year previously.
And if its debenture-holders had reduced their interest to
nothing at all it would have shown a profit of only £1325.
The co. has £1,231,970 of assets, of which £48,944 is
accumulated loss ; £541 is discount; £2O “ money lent
without security ” ; £225 is advanced on the security of
the co’s unsaleable shares and those of the hopelessly
busted Metropolitan Bank ; and £1,080,000 consists of
property which hasn’t been re-valued since the boom.
It may be worth half that amount, or a quarter, or
any other fraction, at present rates—no one knows.
The institution has £1,000,000 callable capital, and
is struggling to wrest some of this from the share-
holders, but, the money comes in very little faster
than tlie half-yearly loss goes out. The British deben-
ture-holder has a lien on all this £1,000,000, and either he
must be paid or the last shareholder be sold up in the
effort. Therefore, the Land Mortgage concern can’t go
and die peacefully as any other bank might do, but must
go on cany mg water in a sieve to no purpose till further
notice. If the worst comes to the worst, however, it is
always better to liquidate ten years hence than to do it
now. It gives the shareholder time to die, or bust, or
disappear, or settle his property on his wife, or do a lot of
things.
• • * *
The Australian Metropolitan Life Assurance
Co., a little Brisbane proprietary concern just two years
old, sends along its balance-sheet. During the year it
collected £4443 in premiums, and including interest, &c.,
its total income was £4540. Management cost £2753
equal to 60 per cent, on total income, or 62 per cent, on
premiums. Considering that expenses are necessarily
very heavy for the first year or two, especially where the
business is mostly of the industrial variety, this is per-
haps not larger than might be expected. All the same,
the Australian Metropolitan doesn’t inspire much confi-
dence. It is a proprietary concern, and the people who
pay the premiums have absolutely no control over affairs.
The whole show appears to be owned by nine people, two
of them ladies described as “housekeeper.” (This item
of information is taken from a par. in Brisbane Worker,
but as the management forwarded the par. without any
contradiction it is presumably authentic.) The manager,
apparently, owns nearly half the concern, and of the other
six male shareholders five evidently have billets either on
the staff or as directors. These five are reported as owning
4640 shares out of 5000. The sixth one mav have a billet
also, for all Tub Bulletin knows to the contrary, hut, if
he hasn’t, he and the two housekeepers are the only out-
side influence there is to supervise the directorate and
management and make it go straight, if ever it wants keep-
ing straight. In a co. where almost all the shareholders
are managing the concern, it is obvious that they can
manage it very much as they please ; the principal check
on them being that they meet together periodically as
shareholders to report to themselves what they have done
as managers and directors, and to cordially approve of
their own doings. The director whose term has expired
applies for re-appointment, and, seeing that there are ap-
parently only seven males in the company, and all but
one of them is alreadj' helping to run the show, and that
one may be helping also for all that appears to the
contrary, he will probably be re-elected. In fact, it
is difficult to see how there can be any opposition. Appa-
rently the first valuation of liabilities, to show whether
the co. is solvent or not, isn’t due yet, nevertheless the
shareholders vote themselves a 10 p c. dividend, which is
a very strong proceeding indeed at such an early stage of
the co.’s existence. This large, generous way of helping
themselves to the funds at the Very beginning, though
perfectly legal, looks as if the Australian Metropolitan
existed mainly for the henefit of the shareholders. _A con-
cern where there is practically nobody to supervise the
management and directorate—where these people form a
strong majority, so that they can appoint themselves, fix
their own salaries, fees and dividends without interfer-
ence, and approve their own acts, as is apparently the
case with the Australian Metropolitan—may be perfectly
sound. But it doesn’t look any too well.
* * *
South Australian Insurance Co. made .£0787 for
the past year and paid 10 per cent, dividend (16250).
This is the first occasion in four years that the S.A. co.
has honestly earned its dividend. In 1894 it made £BB7l
and paid 15 per cent, dividend (£9375). In 1895 it made
£5405 and paid 10 per cent, dividend (£6250). In 1896 it
made £5250 and paid 10 per cent, dividend (£6250). The
insurance institutions down South have got the boom
complaint almost as badly now as the banks had it a
few years ago. Half of them are paying away all their
earnings to shareholders, and most of the other half are
paying away more than their earnings. Almost every one
of 'them is a lot weaker than it was a few years
ago, solely through paying sensational dividends
and making no provision out of earnings to cover
depreciation on investments. If the bad times and the
big dividends and the depreciation last five years longer
one or two old-established companies will probably end in
liquidation, and the all-pervading John Bull will annex
the business, as usual.
* * * *
The Victoria Insurance Co. (fire and marine) of
Melbourne, finished a rather prosperous year on June 30,
the net profits being £lB,OlO. A beautiful dividend of
35'M per cent, absorbs £17,974 of this, leaving £36 to carry
forward. At the same time the directors announce that
they find it necessary to take £25,000 from reserve—
£2o,ooo to form a re - insurance fund, and £5OOO to
strengthen the contingency fund, which now stands at
£25.(100. This sudden desire to strengthen the contin-
gency fund seems to imply that there are more losses to
be provided for in the co.’s investments. The Victoria
doesn't write off depreciation, but leaves the assets stand-
ing at their book values with a contingency fund against
them, therefore the inference is that the £20,000 for-
merly at the credit of that fund was insufficient to cover
losses and further provision was necessary. The fact that
the income from investments has collapsed from £5376 in
1894 to £3705 in 1897 is also suggestive that something
pretty bad has been happening to the securities.
With the reserve-fund thus reduced, and the income
from interest rapidly declining, a management
which scatters practically all the large profits in the de-
sperate effort to pay a 35J per cent, dividend deserves a
fine large zinc medal for its recklessness. Last year the
Victoria was even more remarkable than it is this time,
for it paid away £9OO more than its earnings to keep up
the 35J per cent. div. Before that it was sailing very
close to the wind in an effort to pay 44 per cent. The
whole recent career of the Victoria is a desperate effort
to pay something tremendous and three-quarters percent.
• * • *
The Victoria General Insurance and Guarantee
v„ >. which is a sort of small brother to the Victoria, and
is under the same management, runs on very similar
lines. The year's profits are £3690, and a large, gorgeous 20
percent, dividend is paid, absorbing £4OOO, so the concern
goes £3lO to the bad. Last year the profits were £4987,
and the co. rovally “ blewed ” £5OOO in paying a 25 per
cent, dividend, and went £l3 to the bad. The profits,
three years ago, were £6477, so they have got down to a
little more than one-half, and the business is now a trifle
smaller than it was in 1891. The Victoria General is a
comparatively humble affair with £20,000 paid-up capital
and about £lB,OOO of reserves, and its craving after 20 and
25 per cent, dividends should be restrained till it grows
bigger. ,
The Indemnity Firo and Marine Insurance Co.
(Melbourne) has a profit of only £333 for the year just
ended, and no dividend is declared. Last year it had a
loss of £1328, and no dividend. In 1895 the luckless con-
cern had a profit of £3S6, an amount that was hardly
worth mentioning, and, as a 4 per cent., dividend absorbed
£lOOO, things were £614 worse at the end of the year
than at the beginning. The Indemnity is a bruised reed
with £25,000 paid-up capital, £3500 reserves, £1047 at
credit of profit and loss-these last two items being all
that there is to show for 16 years’ work—and a wonderful
capacity for striking streaks of hard luck. Out of £32,000
worth of assets, it is carefully mentioned that over £20,000
are put in at book values—whatever the difference be-
tween these and present values may be. As £27,800
(paper value) of investments yield only £478, equal to
£1 14s. per cent, per annum, unsympathetic people would
possibly reckon ’ them as worth about £12,000 altogether,
In which case there would be comparatively little left of
the Indemnity Co. In the last six years the premium
income has diminished by nearly one-half, and the income
from investments has fallen to one-third of what it was,
so the Indemnity’s bad luck has been very consistent.
Last April the shares (4s. paid up) were quoted at 9d.
Since then they haven’t been heard of so far as this paper
is aware.
The Southern Insurance Co. (Melbourne) has
also struck a rich reef of misfortune. For the six months
ended 30th June the net premiums were. £17,485 and the
losses £28,116. After allowing: for interest, &c., the half-
year’s deficit is £10,762, and the board, in an unexpected
access of sanity, pays no dividend. The reason why this
proceeding is so wildly unexpected will be found in the
following: figures :
Dividends.
June, ’95, ... Loss £1393 £2500
Dec., ’95, ... Profit 1689 2500
June, ’96, 3431 2500
Dec.,’96, ... Loss 1521 2500
June, ’97, ... ~ 10,762 nil
In four previous half-years, therefore, the Southern earned
£2206 and paid £lO,OOO in dividends, and this paper had
gradually come to regard it as an institution whose
capacity for paying dividends and damning the conse-
quences was wholly unlimited. Since the beginning of
1895 the co. has reduced the balance at credit of profit
and loss from £30,790 to £12,234, which is a pretty mas-
sive loss in 2.J years. It has also taken £5OOO from its re-
serve fund to provide for losses and depreciation on in-
vestments, which should have been made good out of
revenue. The Southern has £50,000 paid-up capital and
£62,000 reserves, so it isn’t a very large affair—certainly
not nearly so large as it was a few years ago. The
management laments a good deal over the fact that the
shares (10s. paid) are selling at 6s. 6d. or 75., and regards
the position as unaccountable. Tub Bulletin reckons
that the management is a great deal more unaccountable
than the state of the share-market.
» » * *
Castlemaine Brewery Co. (Melbourne), one of
the very few joint-stock tanglefoots which have hitherto
resisted the depression, stopped paying dividends this
year. The profit for the 12 months to 30th June was
£2854, a lamentable fall from the £8389 realised in 96,
£12,283 in ’95 and £18,647 in ’94. In addition to the stop-
page of dividend, the reserve of £15,000 and £BOOO of
profit and loss account are written off to cover deprecia-
tion in the co.’s inflated public-house properties. The
utter collapse of the beer business down South is one of
the most remarkable things since the boom. It would
seem that there isn’t a decent, solvent thirst left in all
Melbourne.

Melbourne Hydraulic Power Co. pays a 10 per
cent, dividend; Fitzgerald's Brewing and Malting Co.
(Castlemaine) pays 10 per cent. ; Denton Hat Mills Co.
(Melbourne) also divides 10 per cent.
The stoppage of Langlanols Foundry Ltd.,
Melb., primarily owing to pressure of a small creditor, ends
about the last of the Melb. engineering concerns which
were made into limited co’s during the boom. Most of
the half-dozen big Melb. engineering firms are wholly in
the hands of their respective banks.

B.H. Water Supply people, referring to the
mining companies’ dissatisfaction at the price charged and
the movement towards carrying out separate waterworks,
say that the cos. have never made a definite business-like
proposal to them. The trouble apparently is that the
mining cos. won’t give a guarantee to take a certain
quantity, but will only go to the Water Co. when the
supply in their own dams is low.
J * • * *
With silver at its present unprecedented price,
production must be much reduced; but a disturbing ele-
ment in definite calculations is the output of the metal as
a by-product. In 1895 the Montana mines produced
89,775 tons copper, with a silver by-product of 8,828,1460 z.
fine silver; certainly not a great deal in view of an aggre-
gate annual silver production of 150,000,(>00oz., but enough
to ease the market and likely to increase with the firming
of the copper-market.
* • * *
The half-yearly report of the B.H. North Cen-
tral is little more than a whisper. The expectation on
which the co. was formed was that the B. 11. lode had an
eastern underlay and that the North Central would strike
it. They sank to 700 ft. without doing so. Then they
drove to’the B.H. Prop's boundary without finding any-
thing. Now they are driving south along the boundary,
and the manager thinks that “ good prospects should be
met with.” The prospects are better that way, certainly,
because the cap of the lode is nearer the North Central at
its southern extremity than it is further north. But, if
nothing is found in this drive or in a winze dropped from
it, the enterprise had better be abandoned ; for the sul-
phide ore so far found below the 700 ft. level is not, except
in patches, pavable at present or even recent prices. The
co., which spent, last half-year, £1757 (the items are not
set out separately, as they should be), has, apparently,
£3500 in hand (though isn’t some of this locked up?), and
that, or much less, ought to do all the further prospecting
that the position of the block and the character of the
stone passed through warrant.

» • * *
The price of silver has continued to fall unmer-
cifully, and all silver stocks have come down heavily. A
calculation was made in Tub Bulletin some time back,
based on the half-yearly .report, that the B.H. Prop,
could keep going with silver at Is. 6d. Since then lead
has advanced 225. per ton further, equal to £15,000 on a
half-year’s production, allowing a still wider margin.
Comparatively, though, the B.H. Prop, suffers
by far the most from the fall. If it were not so the other
mines which have been working with a far smaller margin
of profit would have been closed down long ago. Take
the B. H. South, for instance—by some thought to be the
worst situated of the div.-paying mines, since it has on y
recently got to the div. stage. This mine, whose half-
yearly 'report to June 30 has just been issued, in the
period produced, from 41,887 tons crude ore, 12,440 tons
concentrates, estimated to contain by assay 8521 tons
lead and U3,2040z. silver. Now, the average price ob-
tained for B.H. silver (standard) during the second half of
’96 was 2s. 6fd., and for lead £lO 18s. The fall in silver to
Is. lljd.=7d per oz., represents on the South 8.11. out-
put £3301 15s. Bd. That is the loss. But- the quotation
for lead at time of writing was £l2 13s. 9d. per ton =£l
15s. 9d. gain, which represents £15,231155. on the South’s
output of lead.
The South’s net gain by the movement in the
markets for the metals has thus amounted, to £11,930.
The South co. itself is not that much to the good, of
course; because, since it sells concentrates and not
refined metal, it does not get full value for the contained
metals. But the increased value is there, for some one
to get.
The effect of the increase in the lead quotation
is, in connection with the purely sulphide mines, so im-
portant that it has need to be insisted on. Take the
South again to better illustrate the position. Suppose
the silver in its concentrates sold for 2s. (above the market
quotation at time of writing) the half-year’s silver output
would be worth only £11,320 Bs., while at £l2 10s. per ton
(below the market quotation at time of writing) the lead
output would be worth £106,512 10s. Put in another
way, a fall now of 28s. per ton in lead (and lead has
advanced £3 in the last 2) years) would be more disastrous
to the South than would be a fall in value of silver to ab-
solutely nothing.
Comparatively, as said before, the B.H. Pro-
prietary suffers by far the most from the movements in
the metals ; then the Junction; then the Junction North ;
next Block 10. The British and Block 14 are about even
on the transaction ; and the South positively benefits.
The North is still prospecting; ana figures as to the
Central are not available.
• * * *
The Sydney market has worked itself into high
excitement on copper-mining:, and, as usual in N.S.W., the
public does not pause to ask if it is not being: played upon
by quick-coinmission brokers, newspaper tipsters and
others. A wire from the manager that the regulus was
running from 68 per cent, t 079 per cent, sent Girilambone
shares up to 40s. and they are now tipped as being cheap
at £5. Not a word is said anywhere that the mine has no
working capital, but a heavy debenture load to carry ;
that the ore is low grade, contains no gold, is highly
siliceous, and otherwise hard to treat; that iron for flux
has to be mined far away, and that when the total cost is
reckoned up, there may not be much in the thing at the
present price of shares. One cheerful consideration is
that if Girilambone is a great mine there are several others
available in the country, abandoned like it many years
ago. In North Nymagee, only taken up and formed into
a co. a few weeks ago, shares are already at 10bper cent,
premium. The show has hitherto been a failure, ap-
parently not because of the copper-market or difficulty of
treatment, but simply for want of ore. The present co.
has not had time to find any and has not enough
money to work it, if it did. Yet this “ position ”
mine, which may or may not have the Nymagee
lode, has a value four times greater than that at
which the Nymagee mine itself was sold last
year ! It is stated in print that the Nyma-
gee mine was bought “by the shrewdest copper
people here, the Cobar syndicate, who are making a
gigantic fortune.” The inference is that they are making
it out of Nymagee. Now, they have up to date not made
a cent out of Nymagee, and it may interest the public to
know that the Cobar tributors do not expect to make any-
thing out of that mine for some time. They allege that
the developments are not too good, and the ore not too
easy to treat; but they get a good deal of amusement out
of all the great things now being prophesied about North
Nymagee. The outlook for copper-mining in N.S.W. is
remarkably good, but if anything will kill it for years to
come it will be the attempts made on every side to make
money by making scrip. When the public finds out that
it has been buying paper and not mines it shuts up like
an oyster and contents itself by declaring all mining to be
a swindle, which it mostly is, anyway.
» » * *
Tin half-year’s report of Great Cobar Copper
Co. shows the production to have been 1307 tons of
copper, 65250 z. gold, and 15,516 dz. silver. The directors
say they have —“In the interests of the whole of the
shareholders, let the mine on a new tribute agreement for
a term of seven years, with the right of renewal for
another term of seven years. The tribute is to commence
from the expiration, or sooner determination of the pre-
sent tribute, and is on terms most advantageous to the
shareholders, who will receive under it 80 per cent, of the
net profits.” And therein lies a heap of trouble concern-
ing which “ Salmado ” addresses Tub Bulletin
“ The present tributors must ultimately ''"■at
the Cobar directors. If the law allows such a tribute
agreement as the far-ahead one just announced by the
directors, then the law is an ass. Eighty percent, of the
net profits sounds well but is really a bonus of 20 per cent,
for working the mine, and, under such an agreement, the
new tributors could employ as many managers at
as big salaries as they liked, and otherwise eat up
profits. Hence net-profit tributes are never heard
of. When the mine shut down, some years ago,
it was officially declared that it could not be
worked at a profit with copper under £5O a ton. The
tributors went in when it was £3B, and have since paid a
71 per cent, gross royalty. They have also backed their
own judgment by acquiring 36,000 shares—nearly half the
mine—in anticipation that when the tribute expired the
co. would work the mine itself. The original tribute was
for three years, and no provision was made for royalty
either on gold or silver. They agreed to pay 10 per cent,
on each metal for six years’ extension ; hence they have
5i years to run. Big as the mine is, it is possible to make
an awful hole in it in that time. The point for the courts
to determine is whether the directors can commit the
shareholders 51 years ahead, and, if they can, why they
did not advertise for tributors in the interests of the
whole of the shareholders? The writer is ready with his
little tender offering 99 per cent, of the net profits. One
per cent, bonus is something, but the fat billets he could
provide for his friends is a great deal more. Moreover, if
the mine was sold for its present value, over half-a-
million, he would be able to keep the wolf from the door
for a considerable time on the bonus he got to get out.”
!*!***
The Great Cobar meeting ended in adjourn-
ment pending a poll demanded by the tributor-hrigade as
to the adoption of the directors’ report. A feature of the
meeting was the flat contradiction given by chairman
Kelly to tributor Gould’s version of an interview between
them re a proposed renewal of the present tribute. It
is now quite plain that the game has become a mere
cut-throat matter. The tributors are said to have bought
1300 shares at 80s., the other day, conditionally on proxies
for the lot being handed over to them. The trouble, as it
stands, is distinctly a matter for compromise. Otherwise
the lawyers will swallow and digest even the rich Great
Cobar.

The professional promoters of Melb. having
exhausted Westralian gold-mines as a confidence-dodge
are now, to a man, on the “ auriferous-tailings-cyanide-
treatment.” lay. The seedy “beer and oniony” indi-
viduals who 12 months ago would, without provocation,
produce a lump of quartz, an analyst’s certificate of
crushing, and the 3oz. button “ from 7ewt. of stone taken
haphazard,” now bail you up with little bags of stuff that
look-* like Portland cement, a chemist’s analysis showing
5 to 10 oz. to the ton, and say “ there are thousands ot
tons to be bought for a song.” Would-be speculators in
auriferous tailings should note that nearlv every sizeable
heap of rrovedly valuable auriferous tailings in Victoria
was bought up over 12 months ago."

Peak Hill continues the sensational new gold-
camp of Westralia. Stated that 121 b. of almost pure
gold was recently got. from the Daisy Bell claim at a depth
of 70ft., and the vein is said to average 20oz. to the ton.
In the immediate vicinity there is said to be a cluster of
seven claims, the stone from which will run from 5 to lOoz.
to the ton, and “ there is at least. 1000 tons ready for
crushing.” What price these claims two years ago ? The
boom having departed, they are of little interest except
to the owners.

official dinner in Wellington, did not happily
choose the guests. The number was small, but
it embraced Acting-Premier “Jock” M'Kenzie
and aboriginal-native Major Kemp. Kemp and
Knight Buller are the two whom Jock, under
cover of Parliamentary privilege, unjustifiably
accused of all sorts of frauds over the Horowhenua
Block. H.L. s first levee, a few days after his
arrival, was also somewhat memorable,' in that two
one of these, however, had hanged himself a few
weeks ago, and two others had fallen away again.
In conclusion, the young ladies of the church were
implored to gather the yellow heathen into the
fold. (Loud and prolonged Chinese cheers).
By the way, a Chinese parson is this week to be
married in Sydney to a nice white lady, who
teaches Chinese Sunday-school.

At Maitland (N.S.W.) two Chinamen be-
came alle same Clistians last week, per medium of
the Scotch church. The baptism was a success
financially, the collection, 225. or thereabouts,
going to pay the Chow missioner’s back screw.
One of the converts took the name of Jacob
Napoleon, and the other Joshua Andrew. Both
made statements after the hymn, “Safe in the
Arms,” had been sung. Jacob stated that he had
heard the Lord for six months, and was almost
sure that he was right; but he was not questioned
as to whether he kept the spare cabbagee under
his bed. This, however, The Bulletin does not
think any Presbyterian Chinaman would do.
Joshua Andrew said that he only studied the
Bible for three months and two days; he then felt
the Spirit burn brightly within him. It was an-
nounced that theMi.ssion was just struggling—pull-
ing the devil by the pig-tail, as it were. They had
captured five converts during the last 18 months ;

The latest vampire is the type-writing
“sweater,” or broker. This individual, who knows
nothing of typewriting beyond the prices to charge
for work, will employ typists for £L a week (and
women are only too glad to “ rush ” at this rate)
say from 9 to 6, for six days a week. He then
touts among his friends, acquaintances, and con-
nections for work, and out of every one of the
women he thus keeps employed, if business
is at all remunerative, he makes from £2 to jP <■
and £5 a week profit.

“Bob” Walsh, Vic. Crown
Prosecutor, had his St. Kilda
residence burgled the other night
by a marauder, who scampered
out of an upstairs window when
surprised by the C.P.’s daughter.
Some years back Purves, Q.C.,
had his house similarly treated,
with the result that ever since he
has declined to defend any burglar.
What with Purves’ refusal and
the naturally increased virulence
oj Walsh, the poor b.urglar pro-
mises to have a bad time in the
Southern province during the next
few years.

Dear Bulletin, — lie par. of
Aug. 7 about charity on the
Bathurst-road, Last week nine
swaggies called at my place for
tucker. To the first four I gave a
feed. To tlie next three (not
having any more tucker) I gave
Is. each. The ninth I refused,
thinking they were “coming it
too strong.” At 10.30 that night
I found the ninth sitting by the
road. As he really seemed to be
starving, I relented and gave him
a bed and breakfast and a shilling
to see him on his way. After-
wards found that two of the others
had “ done in” their shillings at a
rival hotel. How are you to dis-
criminate ?— Snake Juice.

MY LADY OF QUALMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love’s longings savour of .the earth :
As oil they fill Love’s urn.
The flame of Love, denied that oil,
Could never glow and burn.
The crystal spring is fed by rills
From earthy dens below ;
The eagle finds its food on earth,
Yet dwells beyond the snow.
The pine-tree’s head is bathed in light;
It tops the mountain side ;
But, matted in the darkling gorge,
Its roots spread far and wide.
Yon limned Madonna draws her charm
From ochre, earth, and clay ;
And truest Love has elements
As lowly-born as they.
That love, as Nature made it, take 1
I cannot change her plan,
Nor make a cold, ascetic Saint
When Nature made a Man 1

Vic. fathers of those children who in law
have no father will be interested in a Supreme
Court case now listed. There has always been an
impression that a man was only responsible for
the maintenance of his illegitimate child till it
readied the age of 16 years. There doesn’t appear
to be any provision on the subject in Vic. law,
although it is alluded to in both English and
N.S.W. statutes. It is now contended that the
Vic. father’s liability exists till the child is 21.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Be the morning wash.
I begin to think Australians are the clean people.
Between American and English there seems little
choice. In England, the hotel bath-room only
unlocks for cash. In America, bath-rooms are
many and elaborate —and little used. You gener-
ally have to grub the cigarette-ends and spent
matches out of the bath-tub before you take a
dip. Likewise the chamber-maids use then) as
receptacles for soiled linen. I spent last winter
in a German pension with 14 Americans —an aver-
age crowd: parson and family, women-folk of
professional men, a rich building-contractor, &c.
A hot bath once a fortnight was all the use (so far
as I ever found out) that the bath-room got from
any of tliem. A Judge’s grown-up daughter was
my breakfast - neighbor. Australian innocence
puzzled over a gray line that often defined her
chin, till the mystery dissolved before her mother’s
chance remark, “ I can’t think why Lucy’s neck
looks so queer; I guess it’s this smudgy town, for
I know she washes it every second day ! ” The
gray line was low-water mark for the off days.—
Whhro.
; * * * *
Melb. Customs officers always keep a keen,
inquiring eye on any Chinese who visit ships when
in port. The Chow smuggles for recreation, and
his.ruses are many and various. One very lame
old heathen who had a “ cousin ”on every second
ship that came up the bay was long suspected of
tricks, but it was only found after his death that
the big stick with which he helped his lame leg
was hollow, and held about a pint of opium.

Since the recent Brisb. prosecutions of Ministers
of the Crown and society ladies for illegally em-
ploying “the cheap and reliable,” crowds of
Kanaka “ boys ” have been rushing the trains to
Southport, whence they are to push on the
less than a dozen miles to the Tweed River, and
thus get into N.S.W. and outside of the tempor-
arily aroused jurisdiction. It will be hard lines on
Tommy Tanna if he has to run away from two
provinces.
* * * *
Much to the disgust of the newspapers,
what promised to be perhaps the spiciest divorce-
case heard down South for 20 years has been
squared—for, it is understood, a good round sum.
However, there are other good fish in the sea ; a
most formidable array of Sydney legal talent is
getting ready to shake its wigs over a similar case.
* * » »
The vested-interest demon gets home in
many ways. Sydney School of Arts successfully
blocks Sydney Public Library getting any of the
prose works of (among others) Kipling, Haggard,
and Maupassant ; and now the private in-
structors of shorthand have got the shorthand
classes at Sydney Technical College wiped out.
By the way, Australian Workman, commenting on
the notorious fact that new books (purchased, of
course, as soon as they appear) are rarely placed
on the Library shelves till months after date,
alleges that they are kept off in the interests of
private lending libraries, which would lose money
if works like Mahan’s “Nelson” or Wilkins’s
“ Lady Burton ” were available at the Free Public
Library when they ought to be available — i.e.,
immediately they arrive 1 .

It is told to a Bulletin man of a provin-
cial parson in Victoria, told upon oath—in fact,
many oaths. The reverend had to go away on a
sort of sacerdotal circuit, but returned late at
night unexpectedly, and was let in by a drowsy
man-servant. The shepherd went straight up-
stairs, and, hearing his wife breathing in a deep
sleep, decided to undress in the dark rather than
dis Li-b her. He prepared quietly, and slipped into
bed. The domestic couch seemed a bit crowded,
but the sky pilot was aweary, and slept easily
and well. In fact, it was not till seven next
morning that he discovered the reason why his
sleeping a3commodation had been so limited. His
wife, being a very nervous woman, had induced
the buxom hand-maiden to share her bed. Here
endeth the first reading. Which strikes The
Bulletin as a very funny (and innocent) story.
This wicked paper didn’t expect it to end in that
way.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —In the recent Catholic-
Protestant kick-up about the Madagascar missions
it was made evident that Christianity of both pat-
terns slumped badly under the influence of an
epidemic disease. “ Everywhere the churches and
the schools were emptied, and the people fell back
upon their old idolatry and witchcraft.” That’s
the way Eve always found it. Our variousJy-
tinted brother — believing that he believes —will
supplicate and psalmify and snuffle for all he’s
worth until some calamity comes along and knocks
the Christian electro clean off the same old Pagan
pot-metal. At which thing the only surprised
white man is the missionary. It’s always to the
ungodly that the heathen mind shows most of
itself. —Macassar.
* * * *
The sensational Melbourne “seduction”
case, in which a Sunday-school teacher was charged
with leading astray a tender lamb' of his flock
while on the way to a mission service, fell through.
The tale told by the lamb was to the effect that
she was a kind of moral snowball till she met the
defendant; but this was contradicted by a witness
who alleged that she met strange “ coves ” at dusk
and threw kisses to “blokes” —particularly to
“one stout bloke of a cove” —in the street, and
was an airy little damsel generally. Then the
defendant swarmed into the box and told his story,
which was that the girl was horribly wicked, and
he met her frequently by accident and remon-
strated with her about her sins, and urged her to
repent. Judge Hodges summed up to the effect that
the prosecutrix was apparently a girl of question-
able character, and it was unfortunate that de-
fendant had been led into giving her good advice
for a considerable time after dark. Found not
guilty after a long struggle. A night or two later
prosecutrix tried to horse-whip her moral in-
structor on the railway-station, but they were
hauled asunder before any serious damage was
done. It is a frowsy little romance, and a very
bad advt. for the local Sunday - school whichever
way it is looked at.

the hand of the law. It is too true. The ’Murkans
are energetic in every line of business, except
hanging.

The Marriage Question f
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How long can it last—
So warm and sincere ?
Will love last a lifetime
Of husband and wife-time,
Or die in a year?
Do men speak the truth,
Whose clinging lips tire
Of ardent profession
When lordly possession
Has lessened desire ?
How long can it last ?
No prophet can say,
A bride is a darling
Till, snapping and snarling,
She loses her sway.
If women were wise,
And thoroughly knew
The art of maintaining
A passion which, waning,
’Tis hard to renew 1
The bondage endures.
Where woman is wise;
A crafty affection
Holds man in subjection,
And love never dies.
How long can it last,
This power to warm
The soul of a lover,
To whom you discover
Your coveted form?
As long as you care
To flatter and tease,.
And flirt with his passion
In feminine fashion,
As long as you please.
Melb.
E.F.
Judge Cooper, with the front nanye “ Pope,”
A Pope you are your orbit in,
And Proputty’s thy darling sin—
Before you, rogues abandon hope,
A Pope you are in part, for you
Have learned to excommunicate;
Which is the Pinnacle of Hate,
The hate of sin and sinner, too !
May angels guard my kleptoscope,
Next time Temptation calls the dead;
The Lord protect me, when I steal,
From being judg ed by Cooper, Pope.

Sydney Daily Telegraph apparently
“caught napping ” by a patent-agent and a dic-
tionary-booming concern, who both “got there”
in one recent issue of the paper, with thumping
gratis advts. in the guise of letters of correction.
And the pills weren't even artistically gilded.
H. C. Richards, the inexpressibly dreary
English M.P. for Finsbury, now tottering around
in Victoria, and advertised as having given to
St. Paul’s Church a Bible used for many years
at St Paul’s, London, is the pious barrister who
opposed Bradlaugh in his Northampton elections
during the oath-question struggle. The parsons
prayed for the success of Richards, but, Heaven
having no vote, Bradlaugh won every time. One
parson, after an election, preached from the text:
“Not this man, but Barabbas.” Richards was
“this man”; Bradlaugh was “Barabbas.” The
Iconoclast colossus said that he could not have said
anything half so blasphemous if he had tried.
Richards is a pet of the Primrose League, but
otherwise a person of no consequence.
John Norton, of Sydney Truth, Australia’s
ablest exponent cf the grand bounce, does fall in
sometimes. Recently fined £lOO for “contempt,”
he impressed upon certain people the fact that he
must pay by a certain day or go to gaol. Then he
determined to apply for a reduction of the fine and
went to the Full Court primed with a characteristic
appeal. Just before his application could be
heard he was called out of court. Then arose a
sudden sound of blasphemy in the adjoining
corridor. Some of his friends hurried out to find
John, his eyes bulging out of his head, grasping
his business manager by the collar and using lurid
language. “Here,” he wound up, “here I am,
up to apply for a reduction of the blanky amount,
and this adjective etcetera has gone and paid the
whole blanky line til”

Women that should Marry.
HAPPY MARRIAGES THE RESULT OF HEALTH.
It is not uncommon for men to bewail the fact
that marriage so seldom seems a real companion-
ship—the man and wife are separated by differ-
ences of taste, of mental outlook, of general in-
terest in life. It is, perhaps, even more common
to hear woman blamed for what is called breach of
contract in the marriage relation. If the wife be
delicate, run down, nervous, and irritable, if her
smile and her spirits have taken flight, it worries
her husband as well as herself. Even worse symp-
toms may follow—the woman suffers from sleep-
lessness and fainting spells, her head is in a whirl,
her back aches, and she has that awful crowding
down feeling. It depends on the woman whether
she will permit these troubles to continue day by
day, leading to a life of misery.
A healthy woman is always interesting ; she
passes for beauty because she is happy, and her
good spirits are contagious. Good nature goes
with health, irritability and peevishness with
sickness.
Those who suffer from tho derangements, dis-
orders, and diseases of the sex, should remember
that Gould’s I iny Tonic Liver Pills are very mild
in action, and are just the thing for weak, suffer-
ing women. They clear the system of all waste
matter, make tho stomach, kidneys, and liver do
their work properly. Food is properly digested,
good bloodmanufactured, the skin becomes clear,
the face brightens, and all pimples and sores dis-
appear. Gould’s Tiny Tonic Pills were used a
number of years as a prescription, but now they
are put in Bmall tiny pills. Medioine is muoo
easier to take in that form.*
A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Tl Sydney, August 31, 1897.
Mr Dbar Mtee—
At the alleged sports at the Agricultural Ground
on Saturday, the Chow pageant was the chief
attraction and proved a great draw. The first
appearance of the mile or so of green dragon was
a pronounced feature ; in the reserve there wasn’t
even standing-room on the seats, on which specta-
tors eagerly clambered to see the Orientals
solemnly trotting round the asphalt track in all
their native gorgeousness. Asiatics have an eye
for chromatic, combinations of color, and don’t
give all the bright-hued silk to their women only,
but make up their own pyjamas in orange and
bright green too. A few horses were in the show,
mounted by warriors who looked as if they had
borrowed their glittering helmets from the Fire
Brigade in Lower George-street, and there were
several almond-eyed beauties wheeled along in
black chariots, which, in the off-season for ice-
cream, probably lurk in the back-yards of the
Italian fruit-shops in Chinatown. If they could
only have been worked into the procesh character-
istically-blended with smells of fried fish, stewed
rabbits, tarpaulin and menagerie-shop, Lower
George-street would have been completely repre-
sented in the Celestial pageant.
Vice-regal tone was given to the Chinese show
by Gov. Hampden, who was taken into the midst
of the yellow agony and presented with a souvenir
red rag with Mongolian remarks painted in white
thereon, while all the gongs were banged and a
wild hubbub arose from the orchestra. One
Chinaman was sorrowfully beating a heavy bit of
wood with a stick at five-second intervals. A man
asked “ What’s all this row, John ? ” John re-
plied blandly, “ Allee same God-savee-Quleen.”
The Gov. had to bustle to get to Moore Park in
time to bless the ceremony, as he had previously
smiled upon the new golf-house at Bondi, and laid
a stone out Paddington way. He seemed as if he
wanted to have a placard hung on the Govt.
House carriage, “ This is my busy day.”
The Chinee is very childish, but he knows how-
to dress. The hideous faces painted on the basket
shields carried by the soldiers were originally
meant to scare away enemies, and every item in
Saturday’s programme had a meaning. But the
Celestial knows better than to weigh himself down
with heavy shoddy, and his silk coat, with wide,
comfortable sleeves, is both light and warm. His
man-servant’s dress —there were a dozen examples
in Saturday’s procession—is of grey silk. All the
dresses and embroideries repaid close inspection.
Wearers’ faces, at close hand, looked youthful in
years and old as the everlasting hills in expression,
but few old men took part in the show, and most
of the juveniles looked as if they could trace their
ancestry back to Confucius on the one hand and
Brian Born on the other.
Last Thursday—last night of the session—was
ladies’ night up at the ’Ouse, and the Social Gim-
let Brigade turned out in force to hear the debate
on the Local Option Bill. The hard-faced sister-
hood listened to the smug-faced P.M.G.’s speech
with grim approval; boredom hadn’t thinned their
ranks of even one when he sat down. But when
an irreverent member rose in reply and began to
say disrespectful things about Noah, some of the
W.C.T.U. visibly shuddered and solemnly filed
out, and the remainder darted fire through their
specs, at the denouncer of the patriarch. The
speaker shifted the responsibility of the world’s
drunkenness back to the ark-builder, because that
“ doddering old boozer,” being the original Shock-
ing Example, his descendants naturally take after
the old man. By this time only two listeners were
in the ladies’ gallery, and at these vulgar asper-
sions on our ancestors they struck sail, drew their
skirts together and snortfully withdrew,
The crew of shipwrecked mariners who arrived
in Sydney last week after ten months among the
Islands are Norwegians, and interviewing press-
men have found it hard work to extract intelligible
copy out of their broken English ; but one Scan-
dinavian, who is nearly 7ft. high and quite of the
Viking type of beauty, managed, in telling of the
crew’s sojourn on Sophia Island, to explain how
the chief of the handful of people there managed
to avoid matrimonial complications between his
dusky Islanders and the “ papalagi ” castaways.
There were only two women of any importance
among the female Sophias—who, all told, only
amounted to four—the other two were servant-
girls from another group and didn’t count. Ono
belle was the chief’s own wife, and, to settle pos-
sible disputes about the only marriageable girl in
his domain, he promptly annexed her, for himself
as wife number two.
The Gay Parisienne has lately been singing her
artless begging song among a shower of “ little
one-D’s ” —the Danae cf a copper rain. All this
coin, which is mostly hurled from the upper strata,
Miss Willoughby thankfully rakes up and intends
handing to a charity by-and-bye. Fortunately,
Ada is not very big and the stage isn't small, and
so the pennies nave plenty room to tumble around
her without wrecking that portable flower-garden
on her head. It is impossible that Melb. will seo
the original two doing Julie and Auguste—the
present pair must return soon to perfidious Albion
to keep engagements. Substitutes are now on
their way from ’Ome.
A-certain Australian Governor’s wife was ap-
proached the other day for a donation towards a
charity ball, in aid of the local Sick Children’s
Hospital. Her predecessor at Guvmentouse
always used to respond with a cartful of turkeys,
ducks, &c., and the members of the household
would make up a handsome donation in cash as
well. And they were comparatively poor, while
the present lady and her lord are rich. Result of
the late appeal, a cheque for JM— for the whole of
Guvmentouse.
Also, a society lady wrote to a well-known
theatrical entrepreneur, and begged him to give a
“benefit” for the same.hospital. The man of
melodrama and mellow soul replied at once—by
placing at the lady’s disposal the whole of the
“boxes” and the dress-circle. The lady there-
upon wrote to His Excellency, asking his patron-
age for the “ben.” She received a reply stating,
in effect, that 11. E. would be most happy to allow
himself to be patron of so deserving’ a charitable
enterprise. But—no cheque, no application for
tickets, and “no nuffin.” So the vice-regal box
was sold to a wealthy lady, and people hoped that
some sudden freak might induce JVlelud Ex. to
visit the theatre that night, when the circus band
would have played. But no such luck ! A pawky
Excellency for a piebald province 1 Harmony in
excels is /
“Sassiety,” which worked the Elsie Adair
benefit with a .£l4O profit, made up its mind to
see that the pretty beneficiary got the full value of
the result. The ladies who “ ran ” the ben. rolled
up in force to the last committee meeting and put
up a nice young man as spokesman who an-
nounced that they had been working for Miss
Adair, not for the creditors of the speculator
who ran the broken show in her name. He
moved that the money be placed in the hands of
trustees with the condition that it was to be used
solely to give Miss Adair a fresh start in life when
she was strong enough. The motion went through
with a whoop, and the money remains in the
hands of trustees.
A recent North Coast wedding passed off swim-
mingly until the storm of congratulations began
to rage. Suddenly the bridegroom fell into a fit
of sobbing, and, seating himself on the floor in a
corner of the room, he hauled out his handker-
chief and mopped up the tears. After the lapse
of ten minutes, during which period all and
sundry, including the bride, attempted to pacify
him, he recovered, and then the wedding-breakfast
was announced. No explanation was offered, and
the assemblage did not attempt to inquire.
Singleton was lately the scene of a very swagger
wedding, and has been trying to live up to it in
the local press. The account of the nuptials
brightened a column or two of the paper, and the
poetic level of chiffon, cream
ribbons, jewelled heart worn on the bride’s arm, &c.,
&c., is well maintained, but into the list of highly-
polished gifts, the vulgar facts of common life are
flung. Mrs. So-and-so, “ pair feather pillows ” :
Mrs. Blank, “ wash-hand basin.” The unsoaring
soul of that newspaper man. Two feather pillows
and a washing basin !
Private views are in full swing this week at most
of the studios. These artistic “ swarries ” are
called private views because the room is always
so much crowded that you can’t see the master-
pieces. While the eye of the guest is fed on the
pictures, the body is fed on tea and cake, and a
full-power pedallist is turned on at the piano, so
that the guests’ criticisms will be drowned and the
host’s feelings spared. The invited ones shake
hands in a gush of phrases, “Good-baye,” “Such
charming pictures.” And outside they say, “ Did
you ever see such flesh tints The girl has two
black eyes and a broken jaw,'” or, “Why, that
sea looks like a badly-stacked heap of road metal.”
Ilambourg worship is beginning to pall a little
even on pronounced devotees of cult. Apart from
their gifts, the two musicians are only boys—
Jakoff kept up his 16th birthday by a harbor-
party only last week, and his young head is likely,
so sensible Philistines say, to be turned by flattery.
Mark, considering the amount of worship offered
him, seems very level-headed and is decidedly
addicted to showing his contempt for the grosser
order of adulation. They were chaperoned by
Sydney Moss to the Midwinter Dance at Need’s
rooms last Wednesday, made a sensationally late
arrival, and were immediately rushed by wor-
shippers. Jakoff was boyishly flushed and excited,
Mark peered about languidly with his hair quite
smooth. It is only when Mark is wrestling with
sonatas and concertos in six flats that his front
locks rise up and hurl themselves over his pallid
brow, and he has to wipe them out of liis eyes
every ten bars.
Nicholson and Co.’s recent publications includo
a tuneful and emphatic reply to “Say au revoir,
but not gpod-bye,” entitled “I will not'say good-
bye.’ Ernest Shand’s clever “ Gay Parisienne ”
«mg, “ They wouldn t do for me,’’and a musicianly
Jubilee ballad, by Stephen Adams, called “Her
Majesty.” Sousa has followed up his universally-
popular “ Washington Post” with yet another
martial quick step, “The Liberty Bell,” which
threatens to become every whit as epidemic as its
predecessor. Adroitly arranged and easily execu-
ted bass effects give to the composition a brilliancy
which, though distinctly meretricious, is amazingly
catchy to the public ear.
Waltz music has taken a distinctly more rollick-
ing and joyous turn. Wailful long-drawn-out
measures labelled pathetically “Broken Hearts”
or “ Lost Hopes ” which meandered always in a
minor key and delighted dancers half-a-dozen
years ago, are extinguished by merry modern
measures which must be chased at top speed, and
which make a ball-room almost cheerful. Two
new waltzes, “An Artist’s Festival,” by John
Klein, and “Jolly Fellows,” by Robt. Vollstedt,
are dedicated to the festive description of waltz,
and are both of them fine, bangy compositions
warranted not to depress the spirits of the most
hardened revellers. All of these pieces are
brought out in first-class style by the aforesaid
Nicholson and Qo.
The members of N.S.W. Chamber of Mines
gave a conversazione in one of the marble halls of
the Equitable Buildings on Thursday, 26th inst.
To bear out the, title of the entertainment a note
on the programme enjoined silence on all guests,
which was strictly kept until Mr. Percy «pence
began his lightning sketch, when buzzing curiosity
as to who the subjects were broke out all over the
room. Music was served out by several much-
admired musicians, among whom the Feldwick
quartette scored the most emphatic success. Re-
freshments happened unexpectedly in the middle
of the programme.
All dates from Ist to 18th Sept, are taken up
with festivities in honor of the Federal Conven-
tionale. A generous programme of banquets,
balls, garden-parties, a gymkhana, harbor-trips,
will lead the visitors tpcongratulate themselves on
having a little Jubilee of their own while all the
time pushing forward the Great Cause. The visit-
ing pressmen and HANSARPtANs are going to be
specially feted by their brother scribes at a beef-
steak supper.
The St. Cecilia choir—Miss Pedley’s loving off-
spring—has taken up work again, having assem-
bled a few days ago to welcome Miss Pedley’s
re-entree. A hundred throats hurrahed, and double
that number pattered their toes as soon as the
conductress came forward after her recent severe
illness. The latter was moved to tears at the
warmth of the reception, and the choir was so
husky, between yelling and crying, that little
progress was made, from a musical point of view.

“T. B. Payne, of South Yarra, who died last
week, was probably about the richest of Melb.’s
‘unadvertised’ capitalists. Ilis name seldom
came before the public, and the fraternity of beg-
ging-letter-writers will bo astonished to learn that
the old gentleman paid income-tax last year on

months to think over it—until, in fact, the decree

“Judging from a recent very public street-
occurrence (involving a black-eye, &c.), of which,
so far, no notice seems to have been taken by the
daily press, Australia seems an unlucky place for
any sprig of nobility to form a friendship, how-
ever innocent, with a married woman. It is true
that now and then such sprigs have been unfortu-
nate enough to. have circumstances sadly against
them—as, for instance, in the case, years ago,
when a Governor’s near relative was beaten un-
mercifully with a stick, previous to being turned
adrift in his pyjamas—or was it a night-gown ?
“ The trouble over the balance-sheet of the
Naice Melbournites who ran the ‘Lelamine’
opera calls to mind the last blast of the defunct
Lyric Club—practically the same crowd. The
Lyrics encountered a particularly frosty season,
and went under without paying either the scene-
shifters, the door-hands, or the charwomen of the
theatre, all of whom, it is said, are owed money to
this very day. Speaking about balance-sheets,
another opera co., which performed some time
back for charity, has not, so far, published its
statement of accounts. As in the ‘ Lelamine ”
balance-sheet, some of the items threaten to be
very funny reading ; one individual in particular
persistently charged tram-fares to rehearsals, and,
when it was pointed out that he invariably rode
his bike, he indignantly demanded to know
whether he was not to be recompensed for the
wear and tear on the tyres. Another person, who
brought his tea wrapped up in paper, charged Is.
Gd. for tea-money with the utmost regularity,
while the demands for expenses through loss of
work would have astonished even the treasurer of
an amateur football club in the good old days.
What sacrifices these amateur ‘ actor-blokes ’ do
make in the sacred cause of Charity !
“Friends of a well-known Melb. elderly lady
injured some time ago in a buggy accident,
by which the wealthy widow of a squatter met her
death, are handing round the hat on behalf of the
invalid as her funds are now exhausted. Surely,
under the circumstances, the poor old lady might
expect some little consideration from the magni-
ficently-gilded heirs of her deceased friend.
“ By a coincidence the swell doll presented to a
charity art-union by a certain drapery firm was
won by one of that firm’s very best customers.
The winner happened casually to mention to the
firm that the doll was slightly damaged, and it
then came out that a somewhat expensive orna-
ment, which was on the doll when given to the
charitable object, was ‘non est’.when the prize
reached the winner. Small as the matter is, that
lad}'' is raising Cain to discover the whereabouts of
that ornament, and is sparing neither pains nor
expense to get to the bottom of the affair.
“With a swell-committee to put it through,
Melb. Hunt Club ball (Sept. 3) should be, at
least, fashionable. Mrs. Freeman-Thoihas, who
has already shown points as a show-manager, goes
through some of her prettiest posturings in push-
ing the sale of tickets. The bright little brunette
is a really wonderful butterfly-grafter. When not
canvassing for a charity-show or presiding at a
French lecture, she takes an active part in the
terpsichorean education of the little Freeman-
Thoinas and Brassey children.
“ Rev. Charles Strong, who has come from a
fat incumbency in England, to greet his brother—
Melbourne’s Mayor—ere he breaks away from the
chain of office—fell into the long lean arms of
William, after a separation of 40 years. The two
brothers are wonderfully alike in every particular
as to face, dress, and manner.
. “The other Monday (Aug. 23) when the Metro-
politan Liedertafel safely accomplished its 185th
outburst, violiniste Gertie Summerhayes, in an
innocent white frock and her cropped hair all
curly atop, played a Chopin-Sarasate Nocturne
prettily enough, but found Mendelssohn’s Concerto
too hard a nut to crack.
“ Miss Maggie Stirling, also, was in virginal
white, and wore her soft dark hair in curtains
over her ears. With her wistful eyes, sweet smile
and commanding platform presence, this popular
ballad singer looks almost as well as she sounds,
though something unwieldy in the carriage of
her shoulders and arms stands between
her and complete grace. Mrs. Ridell (Miss
Stirling) has aspirations beyond ballads, and
when the Chamber concerts were in progress
worked hard at the exacting compositions pre-
scribed by Seherek for those recitals. But with
an. invalid husband and young children to provide
for, the family bread-winner has more to think of
than ,her own artistic improvement. The Ham-
bonrg boys took a tremendous fancy to Maggie
Sterling when they were in Melb., and visited her
at her home in Camberwell. There they spent the
day romping with her children and racing all over
the house. At the Liedertafel, by the way, ‘ The
Quiet Hour,’ a placid part-song, owed its being to
pianist Fentum, head musical shepherd at°the
Presbyterian Ladies College.
“ I iie spinsters of Essendon, who invited all
the Nicest of Melbourne to their ball at
the local Town Hall, made the mistake of
issuing the cards in an open envelope with a
penny stamp. Of course the honored ones had
tuppence to pay,’ for on the back of the cards
was written an injunction—the woman’s postscript
‘Present at the door.’ A few cards found
their way back to the spinster-sec., thromrh
vants refusing to part-up when the missus was
out. One lady, on receiving the gloomy intelli-
gence of tuppence to pay on the letter, which
might be only a business circular, refused to take
delivery. ‘You’d better take it, ma’am,’ said
the swift-footed Mercury, ‘ it’s a hinvitation from
the old maids.’
“ Professor Lyle, the Irish don of Natural
Philosophy at Melb. University, is a second Boyle
Roche. The other day, in reminding the students
to be punctual at lectures, he added, empha-
tically : ‘ I shall have the door locked at 12o’clock,
and anyone who comes in after that will not be
allowed to remain.’ Another : ‘Now, this yellow
substance, submitted to a certain treatment, turns
blue, but it won’t, because I’m not going to do it.’
A diligent student is carefully collecting these
bulls, with the witticisms of Dr. Tucker, Professor
of Classics, as material for an academical ‘Pun
Doctor.’
“Self-preservation, in a business sense, was
certainly the first law of nature with the school-
marm who, a while back, convicted her small-boy
boarder of the awful indiscretion of scarletina and
promptly packed him off with a nine-year-old
brother to seek quarters for himself in a private
hospital, The urchins, exiles from Westralia,
were refused admission on the score of infection
and found no shelter until the police were ’phoned
for and arrested the invalid as he stood or sat
shivering on the footpath, clutching a letter of
introduction from his late preceptress to the hos-
pital authorities, referring sentimentally to the
bearer as ‘ the dear lad ! ’ A fuss followed, and
the scholastic heroine, rushing into print in her
own defence, incidentally took credit to herself
for having given the patient his dinner and helped
him into his overcoat before sending him off on
the wallaby-track in quest of new quarters.
“ A certain Australian personage recently dis-
tinguished himself by appearing in a most frolicky
state at a social gathering, where he had under-
taken to read a paper. After shedding a series of
broad smiles on the charmed circle of eager ones, he
stood up quite unexpectedly and cried ‘ Order,
or der, o-r-d-e-r ’ very much after the manner of
Blueskin Royce in ‘Little Jack Sheppard.’ Then
he began to talk of things and sundry until the
company grew restive and called for the distin-
guished guest to l’ead his precious paper. He de-
clared that he had forgotten his notes, but thecryfor
‘paper’ grew more importunate than ever. In
desperation, the unhappy man picked up a book—
it happened to be a Swinburne—and read there-
from a sonnet. The astonished company ap-
plauded, but the paper-chase began again. Driven
to desperation this time, he rallied all his mental
forces and gave them a disjointed discourse on
literature in general and nothing in particular,
after which he adjourned for a long whisky, and
got away as majestically as he could under the
circumstances.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

One of the earliest of Sydney temperance
agitators was named W. P. Crick. “They run
in all shapes.”
Tom Rose, member for Argyle in N.S.W.
Assembly, has just passed his final Bar exam.
For a busy man past his first youth, and ignorant
of the dead languages, the Bar course is a big
oontraot. Rose is “ a whale on detail,’’and should
work up a practice if he lives long enough.

It is not generally known that in his early
Maoriland days—say, 30 years ago—the Godfear-
ing Garrard, now N.S.W. Minister of Education
—save the mark ! —was an amateur oarsman of
great renown. He was one of a famous Port
Nicholson crew.
Of the thirteen ex-Governors of S.A. only
three are now living, of whom one, Hori Grey,
left the province no less than 52 years ago, and
another (Fergusson) 24 years ago. The third is the
engine-driving Kintore, the reigning Buxton’s im-
mediate predecessor.
A Paris journal’s touching series of
articles on “ The Forgotten ” —people yet extant,
who once had a name—recalls a striking occurrence
in Melb. police-court. A sailor was up for a
trivial offence, when it became buzzed round, and
verified, that he was that superlatively brave man,
the Maltese Joseph Rodsreurs, who swam ashore
with the rope from the Royal Charter.
The late ex-Gov. Jervois devoted some of
his engineering abilities to draining and reclaim-
ing a low-lying estate which he acquired near the
mouth of the river Murray, in S.A. He was in-
terested in it to the last. Down to Jervois’s time,
South Australia provided no out-of-town house for
its Governor. He kicked (and Jervois could kick)
and so £22,000 was spent at Marble Hill.
“Lay to your book.” Since he quitted
S.M. Herald office, “Nemo ’' Pickering has, like
another Horace, been living on his farm. He has
succeeded in raising a crop of maize at a cost of
14s. a bushel, and his cabbages had run him into
ss. a head ere they wore full-grown. Still, he
finds it pleasanter than trying to please a sporting
public while John Fairfax’s ghost hovers around
the Herald office.
Backnumber Copley’s neighbors years ago
used to say : “Bill hadn’t brains enough to keep
awake.” Writer, in Copley’s teamstering days,
himself more than once saw William rouse up
when nearing a turning, “Gee-off,” or “ Corne-
hea,” as the case might be, and, before the gee-
gees could round the corner, the future S.A.
Minister of Education and National Ass magnate
would be “ driving pigs to market.”
A rather unusual juryman has turned up
in Melbourne. He was in a civil case a short time
ago and a verdict was given for one of the parties.
A few days afterwards he waited upon the bar-
rister for the other party, told him that he had
since come to the conclusion that the verdict was
wrong, and offered to pay down £lOO for the cost
of obtaining a new trial. The same juryman is
understood to have been on a recent memorable
Sunday-school -girl assault case.
Pope Alexander Cooper, J., catechising an
eight-year-old boy at Brisbane (Q.) Supreme Court,
as to his comprehension of the nature of an oath,
asked the lad—“Do you know what your soul is?”
} “ No.” Does Cooper, J., with all his years,
all his learning, all his ermine, all his full-bottomed
wig, all his man-millinery, and all his passion for
fi ” } cnow what his soul is ? This writer wagers
that he doesn’t, and that he couldn’t give a logical
and easily-to-be understood definition of that
article, even if the prize for such was the woolsack.
Higher Power Brunker is a most involved
speaker who sails around, under, and over, but
never directly at a subject, his favorite expression
being “ with regard to.”
