The 20-year-old daughter of a Sydney sub-
urban newspaper proprietor, during her father’s
recent “ retirement ” over a little libel trouble,
pluckily took over the whole show, and got the
paper out each week. Luckily for “Dad,” she
can set type and “ pull ” proofs almost as well as
most comps.
At Ringarooma (Tas.) a member of the
great Smith family is 107 years old, and
still active. He married at 72.
.A pompous but ignorant candidate for
the “municipal honors” of a Melb. suburb
was asked by a wag if he was “in favor of the
abolition of the Decalogue.” The candidate
sought inspiration from his equally ignorant
prompter, who informed him that “he thought
they’d abolished it in Collingwood ! ” Thereon,
the candidate announced that if it wasn’t
good enough for Collingwood, it wasn’t for
this electorate, so the sooner it was polished
the better for everybody. ”
A story from Melb. Hospital Morgue.
Demise ; Black Lucy, a mulatto woman, pro-
minent in Melb. as a—well—high priestess of
Venus for 30 years past. Among the residents
of Exhibition, Lonsdale and Little Bourke
streets, there existed neither man nor woman
who would say a bad word for Lucy, or would
not have risked three months hard to do her a
good turn. The police had the same opinion
as the old woman, and even the slum mis-
sionaries and the Salvationists recognised the
good qualities of one who had frequently sacri-
ficed her own pocket to save some impulsive,
hysterical girl from going to the devil. “ That
woman’s got the biggest heart in the whole of
the city,” was the private opinion of a Melb.
P.M. after disposing of a case wherein the
black woman was a witness, and his sentiment
was the general one. But hard times came,
and the other day saw the bailiffs in the old
woman’s house. The loss of the furniture she
didn’t mind so much, but the seizure and sale
of an old, blasphemous, goodnatured cockatoo
that had been the woman’s companion for years
was more than she could stand. So she went
and broke her heart, and died in Melb. Hos-
pital through losing the cockatoo.
Windeyer’s acceptance of a temporary
judgeship in Newfoundland is, probably, the
throwing of a sprat to catch a mackerel. Baron
Mt. Rennie is anxious to keep sweet with the
Ome authorities ; and if, by taking a trip to the
New World, the highly-pensioned W.C.W. can
make a thousand guineas or so, and help Joe
Chamberlain out of a difficulty which has arisen
in connection with the Newfoundland Bench, why
shouldn’t the old jury-chewer snap at the offer ?
By the way, what has become of W.C.W’s
volume of “ pomes ? ”

Miss Flora Shaw, some years before her
invasion of Australia, contributed prolific articles
to the Times on the Australasian provinces, and
their general accuracy won her a reputation as an
authority on things antipodean. It was wondered
where the fair Flora got her “ private ” informa-
tion, but one of the Australian Agents-General
knows. In fact, he was her well of truth.
At the complimentary ‘ ‘ smoke-night ”
tendered to Premier Turner, in St. Kilda (Melb.)
Town Hall, the chair was taken by a local under-
taker whose private residence is famous for its
funereal front door. This ominous constituent
threw off a feu d’esprit to the effect that Turner
would have done him a grave injustice by dying
in London and getting buried in Westminster
Abbey.
A Rockhampton merchant, far-famed for
his picturesque and resourceful fluency, was
beaten recently. Speaking of a third party he
said : “ I assure you, Thompson, no language at
my command would adequately convey to you
what a perfectly lurid and blastiferous hiatus
Smith is.”

One peculiar fad of the late Dr. Diamond
Beaney was that every time he received a regis-
tered letter he gave the postman a shilling. This
got to be pretty well known amongst the Melb.
letter-carriers, and “ Beaney’s round ” was con-
sequently extremely popular. One postman who
was an expert at telling a letter’s contents by its
feel, used to himself register, at a cost of 3d.,
every letter for the doctor containing valuables, if
it had not been previously registered.
On the invitation of a progressive aider-
man, Ben Tillett attended a meeting of Brisbane
Jity Council, and made a few lucid explanations
regarding London County ditto. Mayor M‘Master
received the Labor-member with thinly-disguised
discourtesy. Later, he abruptly declined to take
the chair at Tillett’s lecture, or to countenance so
unspeakable a function in any way whatever. It
is M‘Master’s standing boast that he has worked
in Brisbane, as an adult, for half-a-crown a week.
The fly in the ointment of the lately-
deceased Tasmanian ex-hangman Solomon Blay.
was that the outlaw Martin Cash was not
among his list of official workings-off. Blay
always fought shy of a camera, hence The
Bulletin is unable to print his photo., and
an authentic picture of the world’s hanging-
record champion is lost. Every official knot he
tied was duly ticketed with the name of chokee,
and date of choking, and carefully treasured in a
big sea-chest. This gruesome record of ropes’-
ends his heirs will probably be persuaded to
obliterate.
“ Where the Dead Men Lie and Other
Poems ” is the title of Barcroft Boake’s book,
which has just been published in a very attractive
form by Angus and Robertson, with six portraits
and 32 illustrations by Mafinny, Lambert, and
Fischer some of which are little gems of art. In
addition there is a lengthy memoir of the lamented
poet by A. G. Stephens. All Boake’s best Bul-
letin poems are included, amongst them the
famous title-piece; “Skeeta,’’ perhaps the most
popular of all ; “ Devlin’s Siding,” and “Down the
River,” two of the strongest pieces in Australian
verse; besides several hitherto unprinted. The
book will be forwarded post-free from Bulletin
office for ss. sd. in stamps or postal notes of any
province.
Fogarty, M.P.. failed to create a scare in
the Q. Assembly, the other day, though he tried
hard. He moved a resolution that practically
amounted to the abolition of the Parliamentary
refreshment-room. He said it needed abolition.
He vowed it was a curse to Parliament, that it set
snares for the feet of legislators, that it lowered
them to the level of brute beasts, that it led to
drunkenness and drunken legislation, and more of
such stereotyped tea-roics (copyright). His
warmest friends warned him not to go too far, and
to be content with a public protest; but the
Fogarty blood was as boiling nitric acid, and he
swore he would go to a division on his motion.
However, he was so overwhelmingly licked on the
voices that the division wasn’t taken. Then,
alas ! for moral stability, then he meandered to
the very refreshment-room that he had just been
decrying and “ filled ’em up ” for himself.

Aboriginalities.
Dear Bulletin,—You often talk of the
mixed population of North Queensland, but the
southern part of that province isn’t so very far
behind in the same respect. In the ’B3 election,
when Griffith wiped up all Queensland with
M‘ll wraith and Bullocky Palmer and Morehead
and Pat Perkins, I acted as scrutineer at a polling-
place about 12 miles from Brisbane. It was an
interestin'; experience. All through a long, hot
summer day I sat in a wooden shed, under a
crackling tin roof, while Hans Jorgenson and
Peter Henson, and Otto Gross and Paul Billitski,
and Demetrius and Emanuel, and people like that
slouched in, and laboriously marked their papers,
and breathed strange vegetables on me, and went
out again. At last, the show was over, and we
made up a party to drive into Bulimba in a cart,
and hear how things were getting on. Hans
Schmidt drove, and Peter Macdonald sat beside
him, and there were also Demetrius and Emanuel
and Hans Schutze and Otto Gross, and Said
Abdallah—we took that good Moslem along out
Mitt VIOLET ELLIOTT.
Photo., Stump & Co., Adelaidt.
of pure devilment, in hope of making him drunk—
and myself. Also, Paul Billitski was somewhere
at the back. Hop Wah wanted to get on, but he
was told that walking was good enough exercise
for a blanky heathen. So the caravan started,
and drove wildly by moonlight into Bulimba.
Everybody sang or howled. Everybody smoked,
including the horse. Everybody except Said
Abdallah drank at every public - house by
the way. We galloped into Bulimba all
cheering for MTlwraith, and found that our hero
was done, and the floor had been wiped with him
in all directions. The Griffith party aimed a
bottle at us, and there was gory war on the in-
stant. I don’t know what would have happened
to me at that crisis if Paul Billitski and Abdallah
had not dragged me out from under the feet of the
enemy, for Hans Schmidt was busily engaged
fighting a man named Petersen, while Seligmann
attacked him from behind, and so he could give no
assistance; and Schutze and Demetrius had both
been routed, and Emanuel was borne down by
numbers though fighting valiantly. Finally, the
police restored some kind of order, and hauled
Peter Macdonald off a prostrate foe who was
nearly dead ; and then Schmidt gloomily bought
six dozen eggs which his wife had ordered him to
bring back with him, and we started for home
after putting a, large piece of steak on Abdallah’s
eve, which was something awful to contemplate.
There was very little mirth for the first mile or
two on that return journey, but after a while we
began to arrive at various public-houses, and the
company brightened up. When we had called at
six public-houses Peter M‘Donald had to take
charge of Schmidt’s eggs because he was the only
man, except Abdallah, who was pober enough to
carry eggs. And Abdallah being incapacitated and
practically laid up in hospital at the back of the
cart, and being also fully occupied in holding on
to Schutze, who wanted to fall out and was
beginning to have horrors, didn’t count. Three
miles from home a great idea occurred to Schmidt.
“Mine Gott! ”he said, “I forgetted that I haf
mine revolver. Ye vill go pack and schoot
Griffith.” The proposal was lost by a large and
intoxicated majority. “ Den I know vat ve schall
do. Ve schall shoot anyhow schoost to see if de
horse sdand de rebort. “My God !if you do
I’ll break all the eggs ? ” yelled Macdonald; but it
was too late. Schmidt was blazing away promis-
cuously, and the horse started at a desperate
gallop. Schmidt lost the revolver. He also lost
the reins. Peter Macdonald threw the eggs into
space, and they fell on the horse and burst, which
further agitated the animal. There was the yell
of a strong Dago in his agony as Emanuel went
overboard. Schutze went overboard, too; but
Abdallah held on like grim death and prayed in
Arabic. Otto Gross and Billitski were somewhere
in a heap at the bottom of the cart on top of
Demetrius. Then we ran over Hop Wah and
nearly killed Carl Hirsch, who happened to be on
the road, and just then the harness gave way, and
the cart stayed behind, and a maddened horse
covered with egg tore away in the moonlight.
I persuaded Carl Hirsch to assist me when he had
got over his fright, and we went back to look for
Hop Wah. Hop proved to be only bruised, though
he was badly scared in Chinese. Sehutze was
asleep in the middle of the road, so we rolled him
into the ditch lest he should be run over. Emanuel
had apparently evaporated ; and he didn’t turn
up again for two days, and then he could give
no account of himseif whatever. We returned
after an hour’s search for the lost Dago to
find Peter and Schmidt and Otto Gross and
Billitski all asleep on the road in the early day-
break, and Abdallah sitting sullenly on the fence
with his huge black eye, and far up the road an
adjacent settler named Ludwig was bringing
back a tired horse with dried egg all over him
which he captured in his corn-field. They have
no elections like that now, more’s the pitv.—
Titus Salt.

This man, who is mar-

The next size is for the boundary-riders to fill.’

Scene in Brisbane (Q.). Prominent
citizen introduces to well-known athletic M.P. a
Northern bushman. M.P. finds himself shaking
hands with the latter almost without thinking.
Seeing something familiar to memory in his
features, he turned to the P.C. and asked, “What
name did you say ? ” Name given. Turning to
the Northerner, the M.P. said, “ How dare you
come and shake hands with me ? You’re nothing
but a scoundrel. I don’t shake hands with scoun-
drels.” “ I’m no scoundrel.” “Do you insinuate
that I’m a liar ? I say you are a scoundrel. If
Miss LALA SELBINI.
Photo., Barroni, Melbourne.
you say you’re not, that’s a roundabout way of
calling me a liar. Repeat it, and I’ll knock you
down.” The Northerner held his tongue, and
“gat.” Then the P.C. remembered that he was
one of a clique that had tried, years ago, to ruin
the M.P., and failed, and now wanted to make it
up.
That M.P., in the old days, practised medicine
on the Palmer and was, perhaps, the best man
with his hands among the 10,000 diggers. There
was no man, however poor, who could not com-
mand his medical help, but he liked to be paid,
for all that. One day he was passing a Cooktown
bar and saw a very large, flash person pouring out
alleged champagne for a crowd. “Ha ! ” said the
doctor, “ you can liquor up all hands on fizz and
yet you can’t pay me—me, who was fool enough
to ride miles and miles after midnight to pull an
ungrateful rascal like you from the grave.” And
he there and then proceeded to give his ex-patient
the “ father of a batin’.” In fact, it was a regular
fixed habit of his to give advice, money, and
sympathy to poor people—and to cordially thump
every man to whom he had given credit and who
could pay, but wouldn’t. ,

At dinner, behold the bailiff guided to a chaii
fairly in the centre of the “ off-side ” of the table.
Presently: “Have some spuds, old man?” and,
before the bailiff could reply, a dishful of potatoes
went paraboling in the air and landed with a crash
and collapse in front of him. “ Have some mus-
tard ? ” and the mustard-pot came hurtling.
“Bread?” and a loaf landed against his elbow.
“ Butter ? ” and a pat hit him in the eye. “ Jam,
old man, fine jam ? ” and he got that fair under
his gorge. When a rice-custard anointed his head,
he arose and left; nor did he ever return to that
table d’hdte. They had killed him with kindness.

farher, mother, and
brothers all being acro-
bats and trick cyclists it
naturally followed that
the tiny Lala should
adopt the same profes-
sion. Soon it was ap-
parent that the bike
was her speciality, and,
abandoning h mdsprincs
and somersaults for the
equally hazardous occu-
pation of trick cycling,
a very little time elapsed
before Lala Selbini was
able to beat all her ex-
perienced relatives at
their own game. Her
constant practice has left
her able to do anything

day. Neither are the
feats of stage cycling

siastic road cycliste.

A brace of Sydney constables are just now
to the front with a wondrous yarn about a grue-
some apparition and their own surpassing valor in
tackling with base material batons what they pro-
mss to haye been convinced was a genuine spook.
The notion of two large-hoofed, perspiring
Hibernians in buttons capering all over an empty
house at midnight after an aerial spirit would
nave been ludicrous enough without the further
mformation that the thing “ had flippers like a
seal, and that what should have been its head
was enveloped in what looked like bags.” The
whole business—failing further elucidation-
should look uncommonly like “sacks” for the
heroes thereof. P.S. —The apparition turns out to
have been a fox-terrier ! Which should afford a
wholesome publio lesson re the imaginativeness of
policemen.

a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Leashed Militarism.
Cheap blast upon the giddy Gallic trumpet
though that new Triple Alliance-suggestion
may have been, behind it lies the solid and
u gly certainty that John Bull’s Jubilee
gambadoes have materially helped to focus
that acrid Anglophobia in which the three
great Continental Powers are glad to find the
solitary peg whereon they can hang anything
like a unity of sentiment. Nor should any
splenetic newspaper - shrieking discount for a
moment the fact that Britannia’s recent squar-
ing of her mighty Imperial shoulders has over-
done the intended effect, and set a strong
trinity of rival nations hungering for the down-
fall of a colossus that threatens to bestride the
world. However utterly Britain’s enemies may
laugh to scorn the conceit that—
With ruth, and faith, and verity
Her name and fame are interbound—
they are only too dismally well aware that—
The roots of her prosperity
Have clasped ohe curvfed earth around.
And to these roots the united axe of Russia,
Germany and France would doubtless forth-
with be applied with right good-will, but for
the fact that their hatred of the common
enemy isn’t quite enormous enough to swallow
all their rankling grudges against each other.
* * * *
Not quite!—but “the little less—and how
far away it is 1 ” The facing, by England, of
the suggested combination would mean the
facing of it single-handed. Neither Italy nor
Austria —even if so minded—could afford to
stir a finger against three such mighty neigh-
bors ; and the blood-sympathy of the many in
the United States would almost to a certainty
be swamped by the rabid anti-anglicism of the
more. With Russia’s 8,000,000 men, therefore
—Germany’s 7,000,000, and France’s 4,000,000
—besides a fleet-combination that would over-
shadow even her own prodigious naval force—
England would be “ taking on ” a contract that
would speedily furnish her with a new reading
of “ splendid isolation,” even if it didn’t finally
transfer her name to a much less ostentatious
position on the list of nations.
* * * *
But not from that direction need she “ listen
for the footsteps of her fate,” so long as—des-
pite the theatrical inter-amenities of Tzar and
Kaiser and President—the Muscovite’s affection
for the Teuton remains about as violent as the
Teuton’s for the Gaul. If the Russian’s
antipathy to the Englishman is nationally and
territorially sharp-edged enough, his detesta-
tion of the German is virulently personal, and
the predilection for the Frenchman with which
his rulers obstinately persist in crediting him
is a matter —notwithstanding the recent hys-
terics over a gossamer Franco-Russian “alli-
ance”—at which no one is more astonished
than himself. The Frenchman, in his turn—or
that great bulk of him whose word will “go”—
is a long way from being ill enough affected:
towards the Englishman, or well enough toward®
the Russian, to forget for a moment what a
sacred debt he owes to the German. The
necessity for the revanche was created by just
the twopenny-colored kind of Frenchman that
appears to be now again upon the cockadoodle-
doo ; but the revanche itself will have to be
achieved—if ever—by the penny-plain sort of
patriot that is least likely to caper to the
penny-whistle of M. Hanotaux or the Figaro.
* • * *
Immediately ahead of Germany, also, for her
part, is quite as much domestic trouble as will
render it unnecessary for her to go abroad for
a blood-letting. It was only as a matter of
temporary political emergency that the motley
German peoples conferred upon the king of one
of them a titular suzerainty over all, and there
are now unmistakable indications that the
nation, as a whole, will not much longer submit
to the tyrannical vagaries of the unspeakable
middle-aged “ bounder ” who hasn’t br;, :is to
understand that he is a hopeless anachronism,
and that the Germany of the 19th century cannot
possibly be managed upon the monarchical
methods of the 9th.
* * * •
Socialism—virtually Liberalism—is steadily
advancing in Germany towards an explosion
which the Emperor’s insane screw-down policy
cannot fail to render more destructive in the
end. The martial enthusiasm, also, upon which
principally the Imperial idea has subsisted
since the overthrow of France, is fading fast
away before the discovery that too much mili-
tarism is an even greater evil than too
little, and that the nation hasn’t any real use
for a prancing megalomaniac who practically
recognises no nation but himself and his swash-
bucklers. And even the army itself —the
tawdry traditional fetish of his tribe —is
beginning seriously to ask itself if it can
possibly, after all, belong body and soul to a
flatulent prig, with a deformed person and
a pronounced capacity for making a fool of
himself. Also —severest stab of all to a pipe-
clay-souled Hohenzollern 1 his military ad-
ministrators—the men who have made his vast
fighting-machine what it is are shaking in
their boots to-day, lest, having recklessly
created the necessity for its use, their tin-
soldier lord and master Bhould insist upon
taking charge of it himself—with the extremely
probable consummation of everlasting smash !
• o • *
Nutshelled, then the situation is this:
Russia dislikes England, doesn't dislike France,
and hates Germany like poison. Germany
detests England, despises France, and pro-
foundly distrusts Russia. France rather likes
Russia, rather dislikes England, and abhors
the very name of Germany—which last —fail-
ing the abolition of WIRLIAM the Mountebank
—is booked in any case for a burst-up, before
long, on her own account. Probable, therefore
—almost certain —though it is that, against
the Giantess of the Seas, will some day “ come
the four corners of the world in arms ” —it is
not from these elements she need fear any com-
bination that would get much beyond the
screeching, for screeching’s sake, of the mere
political cockatoo.

Drunkenness is probably only hereditary to the same
extent that consumption is hereditary. If the parent is
weak the offspring inherit the weakness, more or less.
The son of a consumptive doesn’t inherit consumption,
but only a predisposition to that disease ; the son of a
drunkard inherits his parent’s shattered nerves and with
them the drink-longing. Some of the most virulent tee-
totallers are the sons of notorious drinkers. In fact, a
drunken parent causes many to hate drink Geo. S.

Forty Years Mining in Australia .
I halted at the gate of a pretty bush residence,
which answered to the description supplied me,
and asked of the lady whom I found enthusiastic-
ally gardening, “Does Mr. T. J. Thomas live
here ? ” Receiving a very pleasant reply, which
assured me that I had made no mistake, I was
conducted to the presence of Mrs. Thomas, who,
in the absence of her husband, who is a miner,
gave me the following interesting details for pub-
lication :
“ My husband and I have lived in Heathcote
(Victoria) and the immediate vicinity for 41 years,
36 of which have been spent on this spot.”
“ Your home is most charmingly situated and it
would appear that the locality suits your health
admirably.”
“Appearances are misleading sometimes, and
though I am hale and hearty now and am over 74
years of age, I have seen a great deal of trouble
and suffering. Why, until a short time ago I had
not known for 40 years what permanent relief
from pain was.”
“You have had more than your share of life’s
troubles then, Mrs. Thomas.”
“Yes. It was as near as I can remember in ’57
that I had my first attack of rheumatism, and
these have followed me up on and off for 40 years.
About nine years ago my troubles were added to
by enlargement of the liver, and this, in itself
more than sufficient to kill most people of my age,
was aggravated by inflammation of the kidneys.
As you may imagine I was in a very critical con-
dition for a long time, and, when just about to
pull together again another severe attack of rheu-
matism brought me down. This brings me to
about five years back, when I caught influenza for
the first time. But to shorten my story, I had
further attacks of rheumatism and influenza again
the following year, and though the influenza
missed me for the next two years rheumatism did
not.”
“Life under these conditions must have been
almost intolerable.”
“It was. But I had more to face yet, for about
a year and nine months I was laid up with a third
attack of influenza. I never seemed to be able to
throw this off as I had the others, and it made me
feel terribly low spirited. My oldest enemy,
rheumatism, kept going at me, and it seemed that
between them they were going to get the best of
me.”
“ Of course you took advice at this stage, Mrs.
Thomas ? ”
“ Yes. I had four or five dootors, but their
advice and physio was not the least bit of good,
and I just felt I could not last much longer. The
pain I suffered beats description. I could not lie
down, and the only position in which partial relief
was to be found was sitting, bearing to one side.
I could not bear anything to touch my back. I
don’t like to think of the torture through which I
have passed ; it was terrible. I lost all power in
my legs. At this stage, when there appeared no
hope for me, my attention was attracted to some
of the published cures which had been effected by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People of cases
which had been pronounced by doctors and hos-
pitals as hopeless, and I determined to make a
trial of them. This was in the early part of April,
and I was feeling just about as bad as ever I had
felt. By the time the third box was taken a
marked improvement was noticeable and this
happy alteration continued until I was at the
twelfth box quite enabled to discontinue the treat-
ment.”
“ This wonderful change has, I suppose, occa-
sioned a deal of comment among your friends and
acquaintances ? ”
“ They were astounded at my recovery and
quite a number are taking the pills, and others
have expressed their determination to do so should
they ever feel in need of treatment.”
“I believe you sometimes go into townT ”
“ Quite true, and this means a walk of three
miles, with a rise or two to face, a very fair under-
taking for one on the wrong side of 74.”
“And you are confident that Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People have been the means of
restoring you to health ? ”
“ I am perfectly confident of this, and have no
hesitation in saying so, and I shall recommend
your wonderful remedy whenever I have an
opportunity.”
In addition to being| an undoubted cure for the
ailments mentioned in the foregoing, Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are invaluable in
cases of Neuralgia, Paralysis, Locomotor Ataxy,
St. Vitus’s Dance, Nervous Headache, Scrofula,
Chronio Erysipelas, etc. They are not a purga-
tive, but they restore pale and yellow complexions
to the glow of health, and are a specifio for the
troubles peculiar to the female, while in men they
effect a radical cure in all cases arising from worry,
overwork, or excess of any nature. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills are obtainable from all leading chemists
or from the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company,
Queen’s Place, Sydney, who will forward (post
paid), or receipt of stamps or post office order, one
box for 2s. 9d. or half-dozen for 15s. 3d.*

To You AS A WOOLGKO WER.—
Dear Sib, —In sending you this letter, our Directors hope to obtain, not only your esteemed sup-
port, but would also ask your personal influence with your friends, in order to secure their Clips.
Our Directors work gratuitously; accept no fees ; being content with the saving accruing from
having their clips disposed of at a rate far below the exorbitant commission hitherto still charged.
No travellers will be sent out to solicit custom, as the terms and advantages offered by the CO-OP.
W. Bc, P. CO., Ltd. are such that it is clearly apparent to the most careless observer that the salaries
paid to these gentlemen come direct from the producers’ pocket, who, if they study their own interests,
will, without the persuasive powers of travellers, voluntarily support our Company.
Our Wool
Stores,
Central Wharf.
We are gratified to inform you that the Company have secured one of the finest modern
Wool Stores in the colony, where the conditions for exhibiting Wool are unequalled, the
light being splendidly diffused, especially on the Sample floor, which consists of a
larger unbroken area (free from pillar, post, or obstruction), than any other wool-store
in Australia possesses. These Stores have at all times been in great favour with the buyers, and
lately occupied by the A. M. & A. Co.
Our
Principles .
As Wool-Growers and Producers, we object to the per lb. still charged to the
buyers in Sydney since 1889 ; for, with a system of farthing bids, a loss of a |d. per
lb. is caused to the grower.—This remaining Jd. to the buyer must not be confounded
with the |d. previously charged to the grower, and which we undeniably were instru-
mental in abolishing.
We append comparative statement showing ratio for the ensuing season between
bourne, Ourselves, and Geelong. The latter place has abandoned the unjust charge
buyers. You will observe that the existing rates on Geelong seven pence per lb. wool
gether to about 3 p.c., the same as our tariff.
Sydney, Mel-
of £d. to the
amount alto-
Our 3 p.c.
Charge.
In selling through us, there is only one charge incurred on all classes of wool, viz. , 3
p.c. This is a distinct saving to small growers, whose produce does not amount to
.£500. We are the only Company making this concession.
Trust
Account.
The Directors (pastoralists of the highest repute and financial standing) have given their
•personal guarantee that all moneys shall be paid into a special trust a/c. As Pastoralists,
we know that by strict economy, such as is exercised in our own business of wool raising,
we can save for our supporters at least 2 p.c. in all cases ; and pay to our shareholders
dividend (not to exceed 5 p.o.).
It is not necessary to be a shareholder to participate in reduced charges.
Our Future.
Judging from the support already accorded to the Company, our business will be
of a large and universal character.
Bs. per Bale on 100,000
Bales saved.
New Zealand affords an object lesson on co-operation. Last
week the growers combined and successfully negotiated a reduc-
tion on the wool charges—securing, for one line of 100,000 bales,
rate substantially below those offered by the companies trading there.
Send fox Way Bills right away. Thanking you, in anticipation, for your support and influence.
Co-Operative Wool and Produce Co., Ltd.

POLITICAT
POINTS
Frank Freehill, Consul for Spain, is in
j^ ris k readiness for next N.S.W. elections. He
has been already defeated eleven (?) times. But
Hope springs eternal.
“ This Windsor uniform
Doth fit thee easy as a glove,
Yet thou wilt find it like the shirt of Nessus,
Which cannot be removed,
But flays the skin oil with it.”
—New Bard.
Three or four N.S.W. Labor-members had
a great go-in the other day. Leader M‘Gowen was
one of the number, and the language used was un-
reportable. The Labor-party, like the Ministry
is anything but a happy family.
The S.A. Labor-party has got quite out
of hand during the absence of Kingston
and his heavy lash. Holder, though always
suave, does not tickle the members’ vanity by
calling them “ Jack,” “ Mac,” or “ Bob.”
Lawyers are proverbially conservative, and
hard-cased at that. But the profession that can
number a Higinbotham and a Lilley among its
most brilliant members in one generation has not
altogether neglected its duty to Democracy.
Six years ago old man Parkes, then Pre-
mier, sent for his son Varney and offered him the
portfolio of Minister for Works. Bruce Smith
was then at the Works, but the idea was to trans-
fer that politician to the Treasury. Varney
declined.
The latest story told of the God-fearing
Brunker is that after having, in his capacity as
N.S.W. Colonial-Secretary, ordered 40 pairs of
blankets to be given to a certain institution, he,
m his capacity as Acting-Treas., ukased that only
half that number was to be sent.
The God-fearing Govt, did one daring act.
It wiped out the salaries of all N.S.W. gaol chap-
lains—a right or wrong act, according to stand-
point. Right, as declining to afford State-aid to
religion ; wrong, as in inviting their services as
honorary chaplains the State asks work for
nothing.
As here forecasted lately, Vic. Supreme
Court. has decided that provisions for payment of
a minimum wage under the Factories Act, don’t
confer any right on an employe diddled out of it
to sue for the wage. They only inflict a penalty
on the employer, if he is prosecuted for his omis-
sion to pay.
One of the most revolutionary proposals
ever put before an Australian Parliament was
that of Prendergast and Maloney last week in
Vio. Assembly on the Federal Bill. It was that
the Constitution might be amended, or any law
passed, on the petition of one per cent, of the
people of Australia, ratified by a majority of the
electors of Australia, without the intervention of
Parliament. This is practically the first attempt
here to secure the combined popular Initiative and
Referendum.
“A MAN MAY NOT CHOOSE HIS PARENTS.”—Bacon.
MRS. SERONIFE: “Do you think you’ll like me for a new mother, Freddie?”
F REDDIR: “ Oh, yes—but I’d rather’d a new father !”
What a depth of treachery towards the
Federal cause does the suppression of that railway-
federation report of Commissioner Eddy’s reveal
on the part of the N.S.W. Ministry !
Foster Rogers complacently trotted out his
‘wealth-tax” in Vic. Assembly Commonwealth
Bill debate Every man is to make a balance-
sheet of his assets and liabilities, strike a balance,
and pay a tax on the surplus, if any. Derisive
members asked if the State would have to pay a
tax to the individual if there happened to be a
deficit
Walter Griffiths, M.P. (S.A.j, hammers
away at S.A. and Westralian Govt’s with a big
land-grant rail way scheme, Port Augusta to Kal-
goorlie, on behalf of a London syndicate. Griffiths
has a tough job. The Forrests have, already, too
many irons in the fire to smile on the scheme, and
the Kingston Ministry is not only indifferent but
fears the temper of its Parliament.
Apropos ß. E. O’Connor’s intention to stand
for Fitzroy Division (Sydney) at the next general
election : M’Elhone, the present member, will not
stand; neither will ex-member Chapman. A
smart Hebrew lawyer is making bold efforts to
run on the Freetrade ticket; but he would have no
chance in a district comprising Woolloomooloo
with its strong R.C. vote.
MODERN CRITICISM.
SAVELOY MAN : “ I don’t believe litter’ture ever
was any good to any bloke.”
EVENIN’ ’ERALD BOY: “ Wot yer givin’ us! Wot
would I do for a livin’, and wot would you
wrap yer all-ots in if it wasn’t fer
littrytoor ? ”
Vic. Full Court is to decide a tough ques-
tion at the ensuing sittings: whether Lormer,
temperance agitator, &c., is not, by reason of his
sentimental bias against the liquor-trade, disquali-
fled from sitting on the Bench in licensing cases?
Gaunson has threatened long in this direction.
Lormer has weighty dicta of prominent English
Judges to support him, and Gaunson is similarly
armed.
According to the R.C. Advocate, an
association “ for the defence of civil and religious
rights ” has been formed in Melbourne, conse-
quent on this year’s Brunswick proceedings and as
a counterpoise to the weighty influence exercised
in promotions by the Orange brigade of Vic.
Public Service. Two hundred delegates from all
parts of Victoria are said to have been present at
the preliminary meeting. The association claims
to be non-sectarian and non-political. The object
of the “Defence League” aforesaid is rather
hibernically stated to be “ to prevent the growth
of religious ascendancy in the province.”
The backstairs flavor of that appointment
of theN.S.W. Attorney-General’s secretary with-
out advertisement or competitive examination,
has been made stronger still by the increase of the
to <£7so within the 12 months, for
more than which time the applications of numer-
ous long-service men for a rise have remained
severely unnoticed. Some M.P. should inquire
into this thusness.
As a political weathercock Dr. Cockburn is
unique. A Federal delegate, the doctor is now bit-
terly opposed to Federation —“ a retrograde step in
theevolution of humanity.” In the afternoon he will
read to the House a report from the London Wine
Depot on the satisfactory increase of the sale of
S.A. wines, and in the evening he will go round
and congratulate the W.C.T.U. on its successful
efforts in the cause of prohibition !
Miller, M.L.O. (Vic.), entered a feeble but
pained protest against the name Commonwealth
for the Australian confederation. “ The name
grates on my nerves,” he said. “It reminds me
of Oliver Cromwell stalking into the British House
of Parliament. ” Anything suggesting the possi-
bility of an Australian Oliver stalking into the
Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords is naturally
most repellent to men like Miller, M.L:C.
M‘Coll, M.L.A. (Vic.), says the Referen-
dum was only tried once in ancient history, and
the result was the crucifixion. Therefore M‘Coll
is against the Referendum. There seems to be a
large hole in this logic. The episode in question
wasn’t a Referendum, but a purely local poll, and
Barabbas was elected at Jerusalem by a large
majority. As another case in point, M‘Coll was
elected at Gunbower by a large majority.
Melb. Paunch :
There are revenue duties which a consistent Freetrader
may advocate, but to support anj'thing beyond is to desert
Freetrade for moderate Protection.
A consistent Freetrader can advocate revenue
duties ; also, a consistent Christian can advocate
diluted paganism, and a consistent vegetarian
can advocate meat for breakfast, and a consistent
teetotaller can advocate shandygaffs on Sunday.
If the Kingston Ministry, which seems im-
mortal, ever collapses, it will be through scandals
about its members or its agents. First, there was
Mayor Tucker—Kingstonian chairman of the
Hospital Board—and his troubles with the Queen
Margaret No. 1 South and other mining ventures ;
then Holder (the Treasurer) and the Thryer-cum-
Drew pastoral leases; and lastly, Addison —
Kingstonian chairman of the State Bank —and his
delicate little trouble.
Robert Harper and Murray Smith were, a
little while ago, the Gemini of the Argus solar
system. Now Harper has eternally offended
M. Smith by sneering at Freetrade —and they
don’t speak when they meet in the lobbies. Signi-
ficantly enough. Harper has strengthened himself
with the Opposition by this revolt against Smith,
and when the leader, Carter, is away, Harper’s
broad Scawtch awccent is heard speaking on behalf
of that side of the Hoose.
Jason Boles, M.P. for Port Curtis (Q.),
has a strong scorn for “those who sympathise
with the habitual boozer and the stuff he guzzles.”
11 Fill high the Boles with Samian wine,”
Is something Byrony;
But Jason’s surname bears the sign
Of branding irony.
His “ golden fleece ” is tea and rolls —
That suck a man should sign as Boles 1
As to Charleston, the S.A. Leg. councillor
who cut himself adrift from the Labor-party, and
resigned his seat in order to fight for it as an
Independent, the Register’s fondness for him is
mainly responsible for the allegations of Labor men
against him. But it ought to have been remembered
that Charleston has been Miss C. H. Spence’s
most faithful lieutenant in support of “ effective
voting,” and that Miss Spence has been for very
many years a Register writer.
Barrister O’Hara Wood has decided not to
stand for Dandenong (Vic.) at next election.
There are already two candidates (one of them
very Fat) in the field against Longmore, and
Wood reckons that a third opponent would simply
make the race a dead certainty for the old ’os.-.
The Bulletin reckons that Longmore is entitled
to stay in Vic. Assembly, and that O’Hara Wood
would make a very bad democratic Parliamentary
dbbut as the man who turned him out.
Of one “ shandygaff” Australian Ministry,
two are Local-optionists without being teetotallers
• —anxious to prohibit the other fellow, but enjoy-
ing their own glass in a rational way, of course ;
a third was born in a hotel, and is now dead agin
the traffic ; a fourth owes bis elevation to a pub-
lican, who planked down the .£4O deposit neces-
sary at his first election and another fervently
curses liquor for no other ostensible reason than
that several of his family drank themselves to
death.
The N.S.W. “Justice” Department’s
game is to do by Suffield as it did' by the Mount
Rennie victims—stifle all enquiry, burke all
specific Parliamentary motions on the subject—
hush up, cover up—do anything, everything to
protect from censure its dear friend the Judge
who, during his career, did more to bring the
name of Justice into hatred and contempt with
the masses than all the other Judges who ever
sat on the Australian bench from the year One.
The Department’s trouble is—not how to keep
Suffield in gaol, but how to let him out.
The Vic. Tories, led by the Argus, are
eagerly clamoring for Federation with a “States’
rights ” Senate. They claim that the “ smaller
States ” are justly entitled to this stipulation —
but all the while their idea is that it is the last
hope for everlastingly establishing a conservative
and dominant power in Australia. The Forrest-
ites and Dobson-ites at the recent Convention
voted solidly every time for Fat Man principles—
and the deduction'is that Westralia and Tasmania
will continue to send Tories to the Senate. The
deduction is probably wrong, but the Argus
means as badly as it knows how all the same.
Mr. GREGORY WALKER, Q.C.

Over 500 prizes were won in England in one-
week on Dunlop tyres.*
* • * •
Here is the statement of Humber and Co. re-
garding the fall in cycle prices. “ The lead of our Ameri-
can cousins in reducing prices has warped the judgment
of many, especially of journalists. Say the pens, ‘High
prices for cycles are dead ; very soon top price will be
£lo.’ Common sense, however, differs. It was the ex-
traordinary demand of the immediate past which gave
many makers the opportunity to sell second-grade cycles
at first-grade price, and the veriest rubbish at inflated
figures. Such prices for such cycles were bound to fall.
Our system always has been to give good value for money.
Our Coventry cheap-made cycles are the best in their
class. Our Wolverhampton Humbers are first-class grade,
defying competition. The Beeston is altogether unique.
To say that high-priced cycles are dead is infantile : the

Blessed be the time which matures the human
mind and the promissory note.
Cleaning and fitting a cycle is easy enough, if you
only go about it right. In the morning, breakfast on
some light, nutritious diet, and drink two or three
cups of hot coffee; after which, put on a suit of old
clothes—or new ones, if you can get them on time—
put on an old pair of buckskin gloves, and, when
everything is ripe for the fatal blow, go and get a good
cycle-man who understands his business. If this rule
be strictly adhered to, the gorgeous 18-carat stem-
winding profanity of the present day may be greatly
diminished, and the world made the better.
Banish ill-health ; self - preservation demands it.
The graceful, select, and staunch Red Bird is ready
at hand. Throw medicine to the dogs. An early-
morning spin upon a really high-grade wheel brings
health and happiness. The old-time sparkle to the
eye will return with the rose to the cheek.
Ladies ! the Red Bird is specially constructed for
your service and pleasure. You will know them by
that “crimson finish.”
We manufacture, sell, and guarantee Red Birds,
and value the universal reputation accorded them by
knowing riders. In the future, as in the past, we will
sustain that reputation regardless of cost.
Amateurs are born then they become profes-
sionals.
About the only good thing which can be said of
some men is that they ride wheels.
An ounce of prevention is better than any quantity
of cure. Better be sure at the start and purchase a
Eed Bird. It’s the cheapest in the long run.
Some say vou can tell a man’s mental ability by the
size of his head. How is it that, you can’t judge a
man’s running powers by the size of his feet? or his
eloquence by the capaciousness of his mouth?
As in cycles, outside appearances go for little : it’s
the inside which counts. If you will examine and
test Red Birds, you will understand why other cycles
are second grade in comparison. Red Birds are
finished in crimson to distinguish them from others.
Like all leading articles, they have imitators. Be
sure and get the genuine.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
For the next six months, or so, we will have to
endure the drip, drip, drip of adulatory' paragraphs con-
cerning the “ terrible ” strength of Stoddart’s cricket-
team. The new-comers among them will be glorified on
their English county performances, the same as previous
new-comers have been glorified—in advance paragraphs.
Mason, Druce, Hayward, Hirst, Storer, and the others,
will figure, in print, as unrivalled exponents of batting
“ science,” and particular instances of their marvellous
“ punishing powers” will be quoted as warnings of what
Australia may expect to witness, when these visiting
heroes get “ set.” The Bulletin doesn’t expect a series
of thrilling displays. When Stoddart and co. get to
work against Australian bowling and fielding they' will
find it, as usual, better than the b. and f. of an English
county eleven, and the dashing batsmen will, as a rule,
come to grief before they have become terrible. Also,
the Australian batting will, as of yore, make a highly
respectable show by comparison with the less careful
English play, and to those lookers-on who are not
fanatical partisans it will seem that either team
is better than the other when the game happens
to be going in its favor. Judging from the per-
formances of the last Australian Eleven in England,
the next English Eleven in Australia will have no more
than an even-money chance of winning three out of the
five test-matches, if Hearne, Hayward, and the smaller
bowling lights prove fairly successful on the hard
wickets. Richardson is pretty sure to put up a good
bowling record, with the assistance of smart work behind
the wicket and in the slips. The others are less certain
quantities, and even if they all of them (taking one game
with another) do as well as can be expected, the visitors
will not have anything up their sleeve. There is no
arguing against the law of averages at cricket. The Eng-
lish averages in ’95 proved the Australians to be a more
reliable combination of good cricketers than was shown
by their performances in the big matches played at Lon-
don. And the fact that the.v happened to play below
their form in sundry metropolitan contests with picked
teams suggests that the same bad luck may befall the
coming English Eleven in test-matches. In which case
they will get beaten quite badly. There is very little to
choose nowadays between the best English and Australian
play, when bowling averages are considered along with
batting averages. But for some undiscoverable reason
the strength of a coming English Eleven is always ab-
surdly over-rated ; hence these remarks.
* • * •
Because The Bulletin has never looked at Aus-
tralian cricket through a magnifying-glass it is flooded
with nonsense of this sort : “ Storer, Druce, Mason, and
YVainwi ight will be a warm quartette for Australian
bowlers to peg at when they have disposed °f the cracks.
What sort of a chance would we have had if Abel, Palairet,
Shrewsbury and Gunn had come out?” Well, the eight
men aforesaid, together with cracks MacLaren, Ranjit-
singhi, and Stoddart, would make a team against which
the “ test” Australian Eleven would probably score about
100.) runs per innings. Against a team composed of
MacLaren, Stoddart, Ranji, Storer, Hearne, Hayward,
Hirst, Wainwrjght, Mason, Richardson, and Briggs (or
Druce), which will be practically equal to any possible All
England selection, the Australians may hope to average
300 runs per innings in tine weather. How many more
runs than that are the Englishmen likely to get?
Thi substitution of little Briggs for Captain
Wynyard (ordered to India with his regiment) gives the
English an old howler —and they will probably want him
every time in the test-matches. Briggs is not as clever as
when he first played to the Australian gallery, 13 years
ago, but he knows every inch of the ground from the
attacking point of view, and can still teach the young
’uns their business on a sticky pitch.
• » * •
No wonder the Australian baseballers are re-
turning. They played fourth-rate teams, and out of 26
matches could only win eight. The tour ended in a big
financial loss to the Adelaide broker who put up the ±l5OO
to defray expenses. A great mistake was made in decid-
ing to go to New York after being badly beaten in Cali-
fornia.
• » * *
Ranjitsinhji goes to India from Australia to
look after his endangered claim to be “ Jam ” of Nawa-
nagar -one of the miserable little Rajput states that give
the British-Indian Government as much trouble about
their successions as if it were a question of Great Mogul.
• * * •
Mr. Gillen, station-master at Alice Springs, in
mid-Au-tralia, thus unintentionally pricks some of the
pretensions of cycling overlanders from Port Darwin to
Adelaide and t'other way “ During the last few months
several single travellers have passed up and down the
line in search of labor. Quite recently three separate
men arrived front the North, travelling singly. A few
days later a single pedestrian passed North, intending to
walk to Port Darwin seeking work. During last summer
a man named Sieele left here and walked to Port Darwin,
and after doing a little work there returned on foot.
These men were never interfered with, which proves
pretty conclusively that the native close along the line
are not much to be feared now.’* The chief danger of
the cyclist, who isn’t an expert bushman, appears to
result from his tendency to leave the track in the sand-
hills in order to find harder ground, so getting bushed.
* * • *
At a St. Kilda cycle meeting, some time ago, in
aid of a distressed biker, there was an amateur race for
three trophies. Of these only the third has been obtained
after a great deal of trouble. The Ist and 2nd prizes
seem to have faded away, and now the officials refer the
anxious winners to other officials who refer them back
again, and so on.
* • • •
In the recent American inter-university boat-
race, the Cornell stroke weighed 1321 b. when he stepped
into his boat before the race. When he got out, he
weighed 124Jlb—he had burned up 7flb. of tissue in 20
minutes ! The public needn't believe this unless it likes,
but believing things like this is good practioe.

That it is imprudent for the very near relatives
of art-union promoters to take chances in same is exem-
plified by the uncharitable comments which are being
made on the case of an individual who won first and
fourth prizes in two consecutive art-unions promoted by
a relative.
* * * *
Wait-a-Bit’s performance in the Vic. Club
Handicap on Saturday seemed good enough to swear by,
anyhow. With Acton showing the way into the straight,
IfVait-a-Bit made no difficulty about sprinting to the front
vhen called upon, and nothing was within half-a-dozen
lengths of the Ballarat horse when he passed the post.
He now looks very like shaking one or other of the Cups.
• * * •
W. R. Wilson won another race with the “ un-
fancied ” Eleusive on Saturday. This filly (like Merman
last year) appears to be about two stone better than the
stable estimate of her form, judging from the fact that
previous to winning three or four handicaps right off the
reel, starting each time at liberal odds, she was not
reckoned good enough to enter for the big spring events.
On Saturday the slippery young lady from St. Albans
gave 171 h. to Koha, who is weighted at close upon Sst. in
the Caulfield Cup.
* • • *
Speaking of a six-furlong race at Flemington on
G.N. Steeplechase day. Tub Bulletin said : “ Among the
unbacked ones in this flutter good judges took note of
Bayonet, a 3-year-old, by Carbine, that may win some-
thing hereafter.” Bayonet won something at Caulfield
on Saturday—it wasn’t much : merely a ±'loo selling race,
but the odds of 6 to 1 were landed very comfortably.
Bayonet changed hands at ±l5O, and is likely to prove a
bargain to the purchaser.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—So Dreamland is going to
“spiel” presently! Well, the half-brother to Mentor
has already spieled unsuccessfully in three Melb. Cups,
and his recent form (when the money was on) suggests
that he will add to his list of failures in Nov. next. I, for
one, have lost all faith in Dreamland’s ability to win a big
race, and regard him nowadays as a “ fly-flat-catcher.”
A few pounds off the back of such a spiritless finisher
make no difference to his chance, unless he has im-
proved to an extent which certainly wasn’t indicated by
liis running at Flemington the other week. Dreamland
hasn’t won a race for four years, and he will not “ spiel ”
for my five shillings until I have seen him do something
considerable.— Sceptic.
* * ■ *
Patriot (Autonomy—Bangle) will be the only
serious rival to Ainberite in A.J.O. Derby, but unless the
race is a muddling affair, like last year, there is little
chance of this speedy gentleman upsetting the “ good
thing."
* * • *
The time-honored Hawkesbury meeting, N S.
Wales, takes place to-day (Saturday), when Readford may
be found capable of picking up one of the minor races.
The Grand Handicap is sure to bring out a good field, and
Gaulus, Sortie, Albicore, and The Meddler all seem to
have good winning chances; the absolute winner may
prove to be Albicore, who should improve on her Warwick
Farm running.
• * * *
The erstwhile Sydney mediocre performer, Paul
Pry, has wonderfully improved since his sojourn in Wes-
tralia, and he put up a good performance in carrying
lOst. lib. into second place in Coolgardie Cup, giving that
good performer Le Var 101 b. A few big wagers have been
got about him for Caulfield Cup, in which he will have to
cnrrv Sst. 71b., an impost sufficient to stop him in good
company.

* • • •
The owner of a certain pony was, last week,
warned off the illegitimate Melbourne courses for life.
People are wondering what the V.R.C. and V.A.T.C.
people would do if they knew who the actual owner and
backer of that pony really is.
• • • •
Perhaps the best lOst. pugilist in Australia,
evidencing in Melb. City Court, last week, stated that, at
the time of the alleged trouble, he was walking from
Melb. to his home in Brunswick. Pugilism is indeed in a-
parlous state in Australia when a champion can’t raise
a tram-fare.
« • • •
“ Jacky ” M'Gowan and “ Tim ” Hegarty, two
popular Melb. featherweights, the best of their class, down
South, met for the fourth time in Victoria Hall last Satur-
day night, to fight a much-talked-of battle of 15 Bounds,
for ±2O a side and etceteras. M’Gowan sailed in, weighing
something over 9st., and Hegarty scaled something under.
The latter looked thin and hungry compared with the-
miniature Hercules he was taking on, but was- as, tough
as a tram-cable. However, his distaste fon- punishment
spoiled what might have been a rattling ba*tl6, and yet
the honors of a bloodless conflict were awaoidfcdlto “Tim,”
to the disgust of a large audience that hadt paid up to 10s.
for the joy of seeing Hegarty rapturously embracing his
enemy throughout 30 minutes of the 60. devoted to the
harrowing encounter. Hegarty is the more scientific of
the two boys, and gets away from trouble as a scared cat
swarms up a paling fence, but it was evident towards the
end of the engagement that in a fight toa finish M’Gowan
would leave Tim’s friends the painful pleasure of gathering
their boy up with a mop. But it is mot certain that a
fight to a finish between these two, conducted on the same
lines as Saturday’s battle, would not occupy the best part
of a fortnight.
• * • »
The brilliant but one-sided match between
Essendon and Collingwood, on Saturday,' virtually settled
the Vic. premiership, for there is only an infinitesimal
chance of Essendon being beaten in the final. Waugh, to
whose foot lay the credit of three goals, contributed
principally to the win, and the giant policeman, Proud-
foot, was the man who played the game for the defeated
Woodsies. Essendon scored 9 goals 16 behinds, or 70
points, to Collingwood’s 4 goals 6 behinds, or 30 points.

AFTER many years’ experience in all forms of
this terrible complaint, the treatment pur-
sued by Mr. Malcolm still continues to give the
same marvellous results.
On May 7th of this year that well-known gentle-
man, Mr. Richard Skinner, of Baradine, certifies :
—“ For eighteen (18) months I suffered indiscrib-
able agonies from Rheumatism in the feet. I tried
doctors, specialists, and hospital treatment, which
cost me over a hundred and twenty pounds (£120).
By pure accident I met you, and you may justly be
proud of performing such a cure. I have now been
well 13 months, and I wish this to be made known
for the sake of unfortunate sufferers who may then
adopt your treatment, which experience tells me
is their only chance.”
Writing from Brighton, Victoria, Mr. John
Bruce, an old and well-known identity, states :
“ I was confined to bed'and -could 1 not move with
Sciatica, tried several remedies without effect,
and, hearing of you, I placed myself under your
treatment, with the result that I was able to get
out of bed in a few days, and am now able to beat
work as usual. I would be most happy to recom-
mend your treatment to anyone suffering as I
have done.”
The preparations used in the above cases can be
obtained, as in the past, at fixed rates. The Lini-
ment at 2s. 6d. (postage, 6d ; ,extjra), and the pow-
ders at 2s. 6d.'('six of each for 2Efe.’ Where the
case is chronic and special attention is necessary,
full particulars must be submitted. In such in-
stances the charge for a course of treatment is
three guineas (£3 35.).
Note where Indigestion is present it must be
particularly stated.
The treatment prescribed by Mr. Malcolm has
never yet been approached, and it is in the most
severe forms of the complaint that his success is
greatest. Furthermore, patients Can be as well
treated in their own homes by postal instructions
as by personal interview.
Address letters plainly as above. Advice free.

Ed. Bulletin, —Re Ayers Rock and other
sublimities (including, for this occasion only, the
most symmetrical mountain in the world). One
can only regret that the spirit of Australian deso-
lation has not successfully obsessed the otherwise
poetic “ E.F.” The Weird is evidently a matter
of “de gnstibus,” but somehow many are perverse
enough to hold that that mere “saline marsh,”
when it bares its pallor to the Centralian moon-
light, to a (say) dingo obligato, is typic 1 Weird.
As to “geological freaks,” what’s the matter with
them? What’s Mount Cook, &u., but a
geological freak, if there’s anything but abuse in.
the term ? Nay, the earth itself as a habitable
planet? A receptive study of the primer of Aus-
tralian literature and art, Marcus Clarke’s intro-
duction to Gordon, would probably do “E.F.”
little harm, even if it caused him to “ get ’em ”
worse than the Amadean paragraphist.—D.F.

Rev. Can in Berry (Melb.)
We have been on the down-grade in enlarging the fran-
chise, and the end of the journey will be the granting of
womanhood suffrage.
Frightful to think of ! And yet, if it were not for
women, what would become of the clergy?
Women are their chief supporters and admirers.
Is Canon Berry afraid that if they get the right
to vote they will also want to compete with the
cloth? Female preachers—if reasonably pretty —
would almost certainly draw more men to church
than the present class of male expounders. A
female Bishop in gaiters would be a pleasing
novelty. Moody and Sankey used to ask “ Why
should the devil have all the best tunes ? ” Simi-
larly, it might be enquired “ Why should the devil
have (on the stage, which numbers of the clergy
call Satan’s Church) all the best-looking legs en-
listed in his service ? ” And what harm has the
enlargement of the suffrage done to Canon Berry,
anyhow ?
Why, 0 Canon Berry,
Like the heathen rage?
Surely, you are very
Far behind the age !
Which would suit your palate,
Which would you think right—
Men to vote by ballot,
Or by dynamite?

What has become of “Willie” Wilde—
Oscar’s brother? Since his divorce from the
much-married Mrs. Leslie, he has dropped right
out of the world, apparently. To be called
“Willie” or “Freddie” or “Dickie” is fatal.
Oscar may survive his trouble, but no man can
survive being called “ Willie.”
Kipling’s latest poetic shriek is a decorous
climb-down (“recession,” he calls it) from insolent
imperial bluster to nauseous mock-humility. Bub
the ingenious Rudyard had a good notion of
what would “ get there ”in “ high quarters.” Sir
Arthur Sullivan has been commanded to give the
screed a musical setting.

Tour Directors have pleasure in presenting the Report and Statement of Account for the year ending August 18,1897.
NEW BUSINESS.—4223 Industrial Policies for a Premium Income of 16591 Bs. Bd., and 338 Ordinary Policies for
a Premium Income of £1604 11s. 10d., were issued during the year, being a total New Premium Income of £8156 Os. 6d.
CLAIMS.—The amount paid under Claims was only £132 15s. 9d.
ASSURANCE FUND. —The Assurance Fund, with Paid-up Capital, amounts to £4OlO 7s. 2d.
REVENUE.—The Total Income for the year amounted to £6044 6s. 7d., including £1504 Os. 6d. received on Shares.
The Director retiring this year is Dr. John Ure, and, being eligible, offers himself for re-election.
Mr. Felix L. Evans has given notice that he is a candidate for election as Auditor.
The Directors recommend a dividend of 10 per cent, on the Paid-up Capital of the Company; payable on 2nd
January, 1898. _ ... „

—OF THE—
AOStraliao BisnjGtl) M'DiOg Co.,
Ukalunda, Sellheim River, Queensland.
(To be Registered in Victoria as a No-Liability Company under Part 11. of the
Companies Act 1890).
Capital, £2S,CCO in CO,OOO Shares of 10s. Each.
24,GC0 Fully Paid Up Shares to be retained by the Vendor, and .£lOBO to be paid to him as follows
The sum of .£530 on the Company being incorporated, and the sum of .£5.00 on or before the
22nd day of October, 1897, in part payment for the Leases, Mine, Buildings, Machinery, Ore at
Grass, Plant, &c.
25,400 Shares are offered to the public—2s. Gd. on application, and 2s. Gd. on allotment.
The total amount received on such application and allotment, less cost of flotation, and the above-
mentioned sum of .£lOBO to be placed to the credit of the Company.
To enable the Company, when incorporated, to have the lease, mine, buildings, machinery, plant,
&c., transferred to it, a sum of ,£4450 and interest thereon at the rate of £(> per cent, per annum from
the 19th day of June, 1897, will have to be paid to the Vendor on or before the 19th day of June, 1899,
such sum to be paid out of one-half of the net profits arising from the working of the mine, or in case
the said one-half of the net profits shall be insufficient, then cut of the Capital or moneys of the
Company.
The Company will have the option of rejecting the valuable machinery on the mine, when the
Company will be allowed £l5OO off the above-mentioned sum of £4450.
The Company will be registered as soon as two-thirds of the shares are subscribed for.
Directors : (To be Elected after Incorporation).
Solicitors: Messrs. WESTLEY & DALE, Adelaide Chambers, 120 Queen-st., Melbourne.
Bankers: TEE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA.
Legal Manager (pro tlm.) : Mr. J. PRIECE CAMERON (of Messrs. Meudell & Cameron).
Brokers: GEORGE D. MEUDELL, 54 Queen-st., Melbourne (member of the Stock Exchange o*
Melbourne); W. H. VIVIAN, ICO Pi test., Sydney (member of the Sydney Stock Exchange).
Agents eor the Company: Messrs. MEUDELL & CAMERON, 81 Queen-st., Melbourne.
THIS Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring from the vendor his rights under a
contract dated 19th June, 1897 (and copy of which may be seen at the office of the agents of the
Company), to purchase lease No. 283, which is for 21 years, of the property known as the “ Carrington
Bismuth Mine,” situate at Ukalunda, Sellheim River, Queensland, 125 miles from Bowen, and 85
miles from Ravenswood Railway Station, containing an area of 70 acres, together with all Machinery,
Plant, &e., thereon, and the ore now at grass ; also, the vendor’s interest in the “ Daisy Mine,” con-
taining an area of three acres, near the above property.
On the property are two proved lodes of Bismuth, known as “ Tarrant’s ” and “ Flatau’s ” lodes.
“Tarrant’s ” lode has been proved to a depth of about 100 ft. in one shaft, and to a depth of 90ft. in a
second shaft; these two shafts are connected, and are 100 ft. apart. A third shaft, si me 200 ft. away,
has been sunk from 50 to GOft., and a’ fourth shaft sunk 30ft. is distant some 300 ft. from No. 3.
The lode has also been trenched on surface and proved for several hundred feet from No. 4 shaft,
making a proved length at present of about 1000 ft.
The carbonate ore proves to extend a depth of from 30 to 40ft., when the sulphide ore commences.
Some hundreds of tons of ore have been won frem this lode, and after being hand-dressed and shipped
to London, had a percentage value varying from 15 per cent, to 30 per cent. The width of the lode,
which is a true fissure with well-defined walls, a verages about three feet. Large blocks are sometimes
obtainable weighing from a quarter to half a ton, averaging fully 15 percent, of metal without dress-
ing. The first two shafts mentioned are equipped with winding engine, &c. As already mentioned,
this lode has been proved for quite 1000 ft., and in Mr. Hall’s report, hereafter set out, it will be
noticed he states “ the lode can he traced distinctly for oxer half a mile.”
“ Flatau’s ” lode has not had so much work done on it as “ Tarrant’s,” being a later discovery,
but so far proves to be the richer of the two.
This lode has also been exposed for fully 100 ft. from No. 2 shaft quite 10 ft. deep, and varies in
width from Ift. to 2ft. Gin., and occasionally being 3ft. A shaft has been sunk 10 or 12ft., fully GCOft.
from the last-mentioned point, and pioves the continuity of the lode, making quite 1000 ft. of line
proved on this lode also. The average quality of the hand-dressed ore sent from this lode is from 15
percent, to 42 per cent, of metal. There is now a heap of tailings and low-grade ore of about 1500
tons, which, on being assayed by Mr. Edgar Hall, proved to be worth an average of 3 per cent, of
metal, and if works were'established would at once give at least £25, C00 net at the present market
value of the metal.
Until this mine was discovered, bismuth had been found in pipes or pockets only, no such thing
as a lode being known. T his mine, therefore, contains the only bismuth “ lode ”in the. world, as shown
by annexed report of Mr. G. W. Kelly-Cusack, the Government Commissioner, and is capable of
turning out sufficient metal to supply the world’s requirements, which requirements, until this dis-
covery, were far in excess of the total supply.
In addition to this, the quantity required is increasing annually, being largely used in making the
bearings for ships and other heavy machinery, smokeless gunpowder, alloy, cosmetics, making of
stereotype, medicinal purposes, &c,, &c.
Bismuth possesses high lubricating qualities, and for this reason as much of this metal is used in
bearings for heavy machinery as the other components will carry, thereby effecting a very great saving
in lubricants.
The plant on the mine, the value of which is .£7OOO, and which is in good order, consists of four
steam engines and boilers (one 25 horse power, one 8 horse power, one G horse power, and one 4 horse
power), one Huntingdon mill, two Frue vanners, one Dodge stonebreaker, Duncan concentrator, three
Tangye pumps, one Blake pump, one elevator, one self-feeder, &c., &c.
There is an abundant and unlimited supply of water and timber for all purposes on the ground;
The ore obtained from this mine is free from all base metals and admixtures, the bismuth recently
obtained from a sample of 40 tons, purchased by Messrs. Elliott Bros., Wholesale Druggists, Sydney,
assayed 99'8 per cent, of pure metal.
The vendor has already been approached by large metal merchants in England, America, and
Germany, who have expressed their willingness to purchase large quantities of the ore and metal, and
it is confidently anticipated that sufficient oiders can be at once obtained to pay very handsome
dividends.
It is proposed to proceed at once with the working and further development of the mine, and for
that purpose to send home an efficient representative to Any land, America, Germany, and France, to enter
into binding contracts for the purchase of ore and metal, so that an immediate market will be avail-
able for the sale of the products of the mine.
Extracts from Reports.
ARTHUR G. HOLROYD, Metallurgist and Mining Expert, Melbourne.
As far as the value of the property is concerned, there is no doubt in my mind but that it is an
exceedingly valuable one. The w ork that has already been done proves it to be a lode, in fact two lodes,
which will eventually join, and ought to be very rich at the juncture. I feel confident that, under good
management, the mine ought to pay its way at once, and, as soon as it is in proper working order, will
pay handsomely. I should call it a rich lode along the whole length.
I conclude it is a most valuable property. In inspecting the heap of ore and mullock lying on the
surface, I found both rich carbonates and sulphides, and nearly all of the mullock will have to be
looked through again, and a large quantity of it concentrated.
MR. EDGAR HALL, of Messrs. Stokes and Hall, Assaters, &c., Brisbane, Queensland,
LATE OF THE QUEENSLAND GOVERNMENT.
Some doubt has been expressed as to whether the ore occurs at Ukalunda in a genuine lode Ussure,
all the bismuth deposits hitherto worked being of the nature known as “ pipes.” 1 think there cannot
be any doubt upon this point in the mind of anyone who sees the Ukalunda lodes. The lodes are
traceable distinctly for over a mile. The walls are remarkably even and true, the parallelism being well
maintained. I have not the slightest doubt about the permanent character of this lode, and that it will
produce ore to a great depth.
The property appears to me to be a very valuable one.
ALEXANDER RIDDEL, ESQ., Mining Expert, Charters Towers, Queensland.
Any doubt which may have ever existed regarding the lode character of these deposits is now
quite dispelled. When more ore is required, there is abundance in sight to be had at a few days notice to
keep the mill going an indefinite length of time.
There is no doubt about the phenomenal character and enormous value of your mine.
Extract from Annual Government Report by Commissioner W. G. KELLY-CUSACK to the
Department of Mines, Brisbane, Queensland.
Bismuth is said not to be found in anything approaching a “lode,” or any vein beyond a few
inches in size. Here we have it in feet from wall to wall, and 1 believe that, in this respect, the Uka-
lunda Bismuth Mine is as nearly approaching a lode as any mine can be. There can be but little
doubt that the lode will continue to a great depth, from its size, and if its quality but continues, or a
near approach thereto, the wealth of the mine will be fabulous. Capital and good management being all
that can be added or hoped for
JAMES M. PETRIE, ESQ., Demonstrator of Chkmisty, Sydney University, New South Wales.
I have been able to form a very good opinion of the place. Firstly, by the unlimited quantity of
the ore proved with absolute certainty to exist in the mines. Secondly, by the comparatively easy process
by which the metallic bismuth may be extracted from the ores.
I can see nothing to hinder the extraction of the bismuth from the ore from being a great financial
success from the very commencement, considering the high price of the metal, and the comparatively
small cost of treatment.
Several shipments of Bismuth Ore sold to Messrs. Johnson, Matthey & Co., Limited, of London,
Metal Merchants, gave a return of 7s. per lb. for the bismuth contained therein.

Tales of the convict system, by
Pkiok Warung ; Is. 3d. posted.
The common squid, little cousin of the
octopus, makes excellent eating, if cleaned like a
fish, immersed in butter, fried in oil, and sprinkled
with bread-crumbs. The head, of course, is thrown
away.
Dear Bulletin, —My barber pooh-poohs
the idea of a shaving-machine—refuses even to be
flabbergasted by the intricate linotype. He
argues that no two faces are alike, and shaving
would be “ a holy terror ” to men with warts,
dimpled chins, or other facial irregularities ; also,
should anything go wrong with the gear ough !
off would come a fellow’s nose. But isn tit just
possible that somebody is already working up
some suoh contrivance to oust the barber-man ?
T.D.
A Bulletin correspondent relates the story
of a kindly act: —
We had oft-times done business with Brown, and as often
had we been paid. It was in Maoriland, before the Ward
burst or Guthrie bubbles. Poor Brown —a mere cookie-
had in my boss a friend such as they look for now in vain
among the wreck of busted Scotch booms. His calls
were rarely made, and then only when necessity pinched ;
but, withal, Brown was provident, and, looking ahead for
the children, insured his life (encased in an apoplectic
neck), and promptly drew his bonus for current needs.
Many a time had I written a cheque on limit-day for
Brown to pay against his earthly existence, and now he
called on us with the premium-notice to ask yet another
advance to keep the policy afloat. It was overdue a day,
but the local office —they wanted business then —was
willing to take the money. Having imparted this confi-
dence—it was his last —poor Brown quietly shuffled off
there and then in our office, leaving his premium unpaid.
There was but one thing to do. I wrote the cheque
hastily, the boss signed it, and held what used to be Brown
up by the collar while I got the notice from his breast-
pocket, and then I rushed off and got. the receipt. It was
done in a twinkling, and poor Brown generously kept
warm until the doctor pronounced him “ just dead.” It
was a tight fit, but the children got the proceeds of the
insurance policy. —Waiac.
“ The tale of the P. and O. Aden is a
worthy tale of City life, with its concomitant cal-
lousness and cold-bloodedness, in the scramble for
the shekels,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent. “The vessel left Bombay with a rich
cargo and a big passenger-list to become a wreck
off Socotra, a lightless island. After cruel suffer-
ings and tortures of disappointed hopes those who
stuck to the ship were saved, though a boat-load
or two sailed in search of a haven, and found it
with an E—let us hope. Two vessels approached
the cripple, and steamed off, not seeing the signals
for help. One of these was the s.s. Voluti, in
which certain City magnates are interested, as
they also are in an assurance in the Marine Alli-
ance Co., which takes all the high-class shipping-
risks. Meanwhile, City circles were confused into
muddy whirlpools. The Aden overdue, nil known
of her, P. and O. absolutely ignorant. On Jubilee
Day the cargo was insured piecemeal at rates that
opened at five guineas per cent, and closed at 80
—a phenomenal jump. Now the underwriters
refuse to pay, stating plainly that someone in the
‘ know ’ had held back a cable, and the question
is : Who was it ? A dishonest P. and O. employee
or a Voluti director? William Rothschild, as
chairman of the Marine Alliance, made the sur-
prising announcement to the shareholders that
their co. did no underwriting of the Aden. Then
who made the money by plunging on a dead-bird
whilst flesh and blood stiffened and died? ”
